{"id": "1709.05308v1", "paper_content": "Harvesting Creative Templates\nfor Generating Stylistically Varied Restaurant Reviews\nShereen Oraby, Sheideh Homayon, andMarilyn Walker\nNatural Language and Dialogue Systems Lab\nUniversity of California, Santa Cruz\nfsoraby,shomayon,mawalker g@ucsc.edu\nAbstract\nMany of the creative and ﬁgurative el-\nements that make language exciting are\nlost in translation in current natural lan-\nguage generation engines. In this paper,\nwe explore a method to harvest templates\nfrom positive and negative reviews in the\nrestaurant domain, with the goal of vastly\nexpanding the types of stylistic variation\navailable to the natural language generator.\nWe learn hyperbolic adjective patterns that\nare representative of the strongly-valenced\nexpressive language commonly used in ei-\nther positive or negative reviews. We then\nidentify and delexicalize entities, and use\nheuristics to extract generation templates\nfrom review sentences. We evaluate the\nlearned templates against more traditional\nreview templates, using subjective mea-\nsures of convincingness ,interestingness ,\nand naturalness . Our results show that\nthe learned templates score highly on these\nmeasures. Finally, we analyze the linguis-\ntic categories that characterize the learned\npositive and negative templates. We plan\nto use the learned templates to improve the\nconversational style of dialogue systems in\nthe restaurant domain.\n1 Introduction\nThe restaurant domain has been one of the most\ncommon applications for spoken dialogue systems\nfor at least 25 years (Polifroni et al., 1992; Whit-\ntaker et al., 2002; Stent et al., 2004; Devillers\net al., 2004; Gasic et al., 2008). There has been\na tremendous amount of previous work on natural\nlanguage generation of recommendations and de-\nscriptions for restaurants (Howcroft et al., 2013;\nWen et al., 2015; Novikova et al., 2016), some of#Stars Review\n11/5 This place is probably the worst thing that ever\nhappened to the history of the known world. [...]\nThe food, however, I initially would want to call\nunremarkable but I can’t. I can’t call it unre-\nmarkable because it is so incredibly remarkably\nterrible. Given this, it is surprising that\nprevious work has not especially noted that restau-\nrant reviews are a fertile source of creative and\nﬁgurative language. For example, consider the\nelaborate descriptions in the restaurant reviews in\nTable 11, e.g. phrases such as worst thing that\n1Reviews from the Yelp 2016 dataset challenge:\nhttps://www.yelp.com/dataset_challengearXiv:1709.05308v1  [cs.CL]  15 Sep 2017\never happened in the history of the known world\nalong with incredibly remarkably terrible (Row\n1),eat here everyday if I didn’t think I’d end up\n400 pounds andsooooooo heavenly (Row 4), and\nfood so amazing you cannot eat [...] anywhere\nelse(Row 5). These phrases express extremely va-\nlenced reactions to restaurants, their menu items,\nand related attributes, using ﬁgurative language.\nThe creativity exhibited in these user-generated\nrestaurant reviews can be contrasted with natural\nlanguage generation (NLG) for the restaurant do-\nmain. Methods for NLG typically begin with a\nstructured meaning representation (MR), as shown\nin Table 2, and map these meaning representations\ninto surface language forms, using a range of dif-\nferent methods, including template-based gener-\nation, statistically trained linguistically-informed\nNLG engines, and neural approaches (Bangalore\nand Rambow, 2000; Walker and Rambow, 2002).\nThese approaches vary in the degree to which they\ncan generate syntactically and semantically cor-\nrect utterances, but in most cases the stylistic vari-\nation they can generate is extremely limited. Ta-\nble 2 illustrates sample restaurant domain utter-\nances produced by recent statistical/neural natu-\nral language generators (Higashinaka et al., 2007a;\nMairesse and Walker, 2007; Wen et al., 2015;\nNovikova et al., 2016; Dusek and Jurc ´ıcek, 2016).\nOne of the most prominent characteristics of\nrestaurant reviews in the Yelp corpus is the preva-\nlent use of hyperbolic language, such as the phrase\n“incredibly remarkably terrible” in Table 1. Hy-\nperbole is often found in persuasive language, and\nis classiﬁed as a form of ﬁgurative language (Mc-\nCarthy and Carter, 2004; Cano Mora, 2009). Col-\nston and O’Brien describe how an event or situa-\ntion evokes a scale, and how hyperbole exagger-\nates a literal situation, introducing a discrepancy\nbetween the “truth” and what is said (Colston and\nKeller, 1998; Colston and O’Brien, 2000). Hy-\nperbole moves the strength of a statement up and\ndown the scale, away from the literal meaning,\nwhere the degree of movement reﬂects the degree\nof contrast or exaggeration. Depending on what\nthey modify, adverbial intensiﬁers like totally, ab-\nsolutely, andincredibly can shift the strength of the\nassertion to extreme negative or positive.\nSimilarly, Kreuz and Roberts (1995) describe\na standard frame for hyperbole in English where\nan adverb modiﬁes an extreme, positive adjective,\ne.g. “That was absolutely amazing !”or“Thatwassimply the most incredible dining experi-\nence in my entire life. ” Such frames can be seen\nin the reviews in Table 1, but we also see many\nother idiomatic hyperbolic expressions such as out\nof this world (Cano Mora, 2009).\nOur goal is to develop a natural language gen-\nerator for the restaurant domain that can harvest\nand make use of these types of stylistic variations.\nWe explore a data-driven approach to automati-\ncally select stylistically varied utterances in the\nrestaurant review domain as candidates for review\nconstruction. We empirically learn hyperbolic ad-\njective patterns that are highly correlated with two\nclasses (positive and negative reviews). Using dif-\nferent resources, we also identify and delexicalize\nrestaurant, cuisine, food, service, andstaff enti-\nties, and select short, single-entity utterances that\nare simple to templatize.\nOur overall approach is thus similar to Hi-\ngashinaka et al. (2007a,b), who describe a method\nfor harvesting an NLG dictionary from restaurant\nreviews, however our focus on learning expressive\nlanguage, in particular hyperbole as a type of ﬁgu-\nrative language, is novel. Our framework consists\nof the following steps:\n1. Collect a large number of strongly positive\nand strongly negative reviews in the restau-\nrant domain;\n2. Use a linguistic pattern learner to identify lin-\nguistic frames that use hyperbole;\n3. Create generation templates from the iden-\ntiﬁed linguistic patterns and infer their con-\ntexts of use;\n4. Learn to rank the generation templates for\nconvincingness and quality.\nWe see Steps 1 to 3 as the overgeneration phase,\naimed at vastly expanding the types of stylistic\nvariation possible, while Step 4 is the ranking\nphase, in a classic overgenerate and rank NLG ar-\nchitecture (Langkilde and Knight, 1998; Rambow\net al., 2001). We focus in this paper on Steps 1\nto 3, expecting to improve these steps before we\nmove on to Step 4.\nThus, in this paper, we conducted an evalua-\ntion experiment to compare three different types\nof NLG templates: pre-deﬁned BASIC templates\nsimilar to those used in current NLG engines for\nthe restaurant domain (Walker et al., 2007; Wen\net al., 2015), the basic templates stylized with\nMR NLG System\nname[Emilios]\nfoodquality[excellent]\nlocation[city centre]\ncuisine[Italian]\ndecor[decent]\nservice[decent]Emilios decor and service are both decent, but its food quality\nis nothing short of excellent. It serves Italian food and its in\nthe City Centre.Seq2Seq NLG\n(Nayak et al., 2017)\nname[The Eagle]\neattype[coffee shop]\npricerange[moderate]\ncustomerrating[3/5]\nkidsFriendly[yes]\nnear[Burger King]The three star coffee shop, The Eagle, gives families a mid-\npriced dining experience featuring a variety of wines and\ncheeses. Find The Eagle near Burger King.Human annotators\n(Novikova et al., 2016)\nname[X]\ntype[placetoeat]\nnear[Y]\nfood[Continental]\nfood[French]X is a restaurant providing french and continental food, near\nY.Seq2Seq Generation\n(Dusek and Jurc ´ıcek, 2016)\nname[Red door cafe]\ngoodformeal[breakfast]\narea[cathedral hill]\nkidsallowed[no]Red door cafe is a good restaurant for breakfast in the area of\ncathedral hill and does not allow children .LSTM Generation\n(Wen et al., 2015)\nname[Le Marais]\ncuisine[French]\nfoodquality[good]\nservice[good]\ndecor[decent]\nprice[$44]I am sure you would like Le Marais, you know. The atmo-\nsphere is acceptable, the servers are nice and it’s a fresh,\nkosher and steak house place. Actually, the food is good, even\nif its price is 44 dollars.PERSONAGE\n(Mairesse and Walker, 2007)\nname[Babbo]\nfoodquality[superb]\nservice[excellent]\ndecor[superb]The food is phenomenal and the atmosphere is very unique.\nBabbo has excellent service. It has the best overall quality\namong the selected restaurants.Unsupervised Method for\nLexicon Learning\n(Higashinaka et al., 2007a)\nTable 2: Example Meaning Representations (MR) and Corresponding Natural Language Generation\n(NLG) Output in the Restaurant Domain\nour learned patterns for more HYPERBOLIC tem-\nplates, and ﬁnally a class of CREATIVE templates\nthat incorporate full sentence templates from user\nreviews. Our expectation was that many of the\nCREATIVE templates would fail to be appropriate\nto their contexts, but that our HYPERBOLIC tem-\nplates would be both appropriate and more inter-\nesting and convincing than the BASIC templates.\nHowever, our results show that our creative tem-\nplates are preferred as more convincing, interest-\ning, and natural across the board. We discuss how\nwe can use quantitative metrics associated with the\nlearned templates for future ranking, and analyze\ncharacteristic linguistic categories in each class.\n2 Data\nOur restaurant review data comes from the Yelp\ndataset challenge, which includes 144K busi-\nnesses with over 4.1M reviews. We randomly se-\nlect 10K businesses located in the US that are clas-\nsiﬁed as restaurants, resulting in a set of around\n40K reviews. The data consists of around 4K 1stars, 3.8K 2 stars, 5.6K 3 stars, 11.3K 4 stars, and\n15K 5 stars. We divide the reviews by stars, and\ncreate three datasets: negative (using all of the 1-2\nstars), positive (balancing the number of negative\nreviews using the 5 stars), and neutral (using all of\nthe 3 stars). Table 3 shows our data distribution.\nSplit Stars Num Reviews\nPOSITIVE 5 7,853\nNEUTRAL 3 5,610\nNEGATIVE 1-2 7,853\nTable 3: Selected Review Data Distribution\n3 Learning Patterns for Hyperbole\nOur goal is to learn patterns that are highly asso-\nciated with the extreme positive and negative re-\nviews, and that exemplify strong, expressive lan-\nguage. To automatically learn such patterns, we\nuse the AutoSlog-TS weakly-supervised extrac-\ntion pattern learner (Riloff, 1996).\nAutoSlog-TS uses a set of syntactic templates\nto learn lexically-grounded patterns. AutoSlog\ndoes not require ﬁne-grained labels on training\ndata: all it requires is that the training data be di-\nvided into two distinct classes. Here, we run two\nseparate AutoSlog experiments, one in which the\nclasses are POSITIVE compared to NEUTRAL , and\nthe other where the NEGATIVE class is compared\ntoNEUTRAL . We hypothesize that in this way,\nwe can surface the most commonly used patterns\nfrom each class that are not necessarily sentiment-\nrelated.\nAutoSlog applies the Sundance shallow parser\n(Riloff and Phillips, 2004) to each sentence of\neach review, ﬁnds all possible matches for its syn-\ntactic templates, and then instantiates the syntactic\ntemplates with the words in the sentence to pro-\nduce a speciﬁc lexico-syntactic expression. Most\nimportantly, it uses the labels associated with the\ndata to compute statistics for how frequently each\npattern occurs in each class. Thus, for each pat-\nternp, we learn the P( POSITIVE /NEGATIVEjp),\nthe P( NEUTRALjp), and the pattern’s frequency.\nTable 4 shows examples of the patterns we learn\nand sample instantiations, with their respective\nfrequency (F) and probabilities (P). In the pattern\ntemplate column of Table 4, PassVP refers to pas-\nsive voice verb phrases (VPs), ActVP refers to\nactive voice VPs, InfVP refers to inﬁnitive VPs,\nand AuxVP refers to VPs where the main verb is\na form of to be orto have . Subjects (subj), di-\nrect objects (dobj), noun phrases (np), and posses-\nsives (genitives) can also be extracted by the pat-\nterns. Because we are particularly interested in de-\nscriptive patterns, we also use ngram pattern tem-\nplates, AdjAdj, AdvAdj, AdvAdvAdj , as\nin related work (Oraby et al., 2015, 2016).\nOur goal is to ﬁnd highly reliable patterns with-\nout sacriﬁcing linguistic variation. Current statis-\ntical methods for training NLG engines typically\neliminate linguistic variability by seeking to learn\nstandard, more generic patterns that occur fre-\nquently in the data (Liu et al., 2016; Nayak et al.,\n2017). Since this phase of our work aims to vastly\nexpand the amount of linguistic variation possible,\nwe select instantiations that have a frequency of at\nleast 3, and a probability of at least 0.75 associa-\ntion with the respective class (Oraby et al., 2015,\n2016). We hypothesize that patterns that occur at\nleast 3 times should be fairly reliable, and those\nthat have at least a 75% probability of being as-\nsociated with the positive or negative class shouldF P Pattern Template Example Pattern\nPositive\n40 1.0 <subj>ActInfVP Dobj <subj>wait come\n19 1.0 ActVP Prep <Np> tucked in <Np>\n54 0.9 AdjAdj hands down\n30 0.9 <subj>ActVP Dobj <subj>worth wait\n20 0.9 NpPrep <Np> screaming for\n10 0.9 <subj>AuxVP Adj <subj>be scrumptious\n416 0.8 AdjNoun great food\n16 0.8 PassVP Prep NP addicted to\n113 0.7 AdvAdj very fresh\n4 0.7 AdjNoun go-to restaurant\nNegative\n17 1.0 <subj>AuxVP Adj <subj>be impossible\n13 1.0 AdjNoun negative stars\n12 1.0 <subj>ActVP Dobj <subj>got poisoning\n23 0.9 AdjNoun no sense\n134 0.8 <subj>AuxVP Adj <subj>be awful\n26 0.8 <subj>AuxVP Adj <subj>be rubbery\n19 0.8 <subj>ActVP <subj>not waste\n107 0.8 AdjNoun poor service\n100 0.8 AdjNoun no way\n201 0.8 <subj>AuxVP Adj <subj>not be back\nTable 4: Examples of Pattern Templates in\nAutoSlog-TS and Instantiations by Class\nbe distinctive. Using these ﬁlters, we learn 8,320\npositive adjective patterns, and 7,839 negative ad-\njective patterns.\nWe also observe that patterns learned using\nstricter thresholds (for example, frequency of at\nleast 10 and probability of at least 0.9) also gives\nus useful patterns, and note that we can use the fre-\nquencies and probabilities in our future rank task.\nFor larger coverage, we experiment with our less\nrestrictive thresholds in the current work.\n4 Designing Review Templates\nTo make use of the descriptive adjective patterns\nwe learned, we needed to ﬁrst identify what en-\ntities each of the patterns describes. To do this,\nwe aggregate lexicons for each of ﬁve important\nrestaurant entities: restaurant-type, cuisine, food,\nservice, andstaff using Wikipedia2and DBpedia3.\nWe end up with 14 items for restaurant-types (e.g.\n“cafe”), 45 for cuisines (e.g. “Italian”), 4,913 for\nfoods and ingredients (e.g. “sushi”), 12 for staff\n(e.g. “waiters”), and 2 for service (e.g. “customer\nservice”).\n4.1 Basic Templates\nTo construct the most basic set of templates, we\nuse simple relationships between adjectives and\nthe entities they describe to deﬁne a set of sen-\ntences with entity slots, i.e. “They had [adj] (en-\ntity). ” ,“The (entity) wasjis [adj]. ” ,“The (entity)\n2https://www.wikipedia.org/\n3http://wiki.dbpedia.org/\nlookedjtasted [adj]. ” We use basic lists of adjec-\ntives commonly found in reviews for these base-\nline templates. To vary the templates, we alternate\nbetween using only simple sentences, and some-\ntimes combine related entities into more complex\nsentences (e.g. service andstaff, or restaurant-\ntype andcuisine ).\n4.2 Hyperbolic Templates\nFor our hyperbolic templates, we replace the stan-\ndard adjectives in the basic templates with adjec-\ntive patterns learned from the restaurant reviews.\nTo select appropriate adjectives patterns for re-\nplacement in each basic template, we ﬁrst delex-\nicalize the sentences that instantiate our learned\nadjective patterns for each class, and create sets\nof (entity, adjective pattern) pairs based on the\nrelationship between the adjective and the entity\n(“is”, “was”, “tasted”, etc.), as above. Using this\nmethod, we collect 37 restaurant, 30 cuisine, 247\nfood, 45 service, and 56 staff patterns for positive\nand 18 restaurant, 9 cuisine, 221 food, 75 service,\nand 61 staff patterns for negative. Table 5 shows\nexample patterns in each class for the food and\nstaff entity types.\n4.3 Creative Templates\nFinally, for our creative templates, we sample\nfrom our set of delexicalized sentences for each\nentity type, as long as they:\n\u000fcontain a single AutoSlog adjective pattern\n\u000fcontain a single identiﬁable entity type\n\u000fare between 5-15 words long\nWe enforce these limitations to gather sim-\nple sentences that are short enough to templa-\ntize. Thus, we end up with sentence templates for\neach entity type for both the positive and negative\nclasses, collecting 146 restaurant, 61 cuisine, 743\nfood, 90 service, and 144 staff patterns for posi-\ntive and 45 restaurant, 12 cuisine, 480 food, 126\nservice, and 89 staff patterns for negative. Table\n6 shows examples of our templatized sentences\nfor the positive and negative classes, with their\nAutoSlog-TS adjective patterns between brackets,\nand capitalized subject extractions when applica-\nble. Since\nour focus here is on overgeneration, we include\nthese and evaluate their quality. Table 7 shows ex-\namples of each template type we create.\n5 Evaluating Template Styles\nIn order to evaluate our template variations,\nwe choose to focus on three particular criteria:\nconvincingness, interestingness, andnaturalness .\nWe evaluate convincingness because creative lan-\nguage such as hyperbole is often used in persua-\nsive language, along with other ﬁgurative forms\n(Kreuz and Roberts, 1995). Naturalness is an im-\nportant concern in generation, so we are also inter-\nested in the comparison between the perceived nat-\nuralness of each variation style, and we hypothe-\nsize that interestingness would increase as we used\nEntity Template\nPositive\nRESTAURANT By [FAR MY] favorite <RESTAURANT ENTITY >I HA VE EVER been to in my life .\nCUISINE Wow what a great [LITTLE <CUISINE ENTITY >] joint !\nFOOD The<FOOD ENTITY >is not cheap , but [WELL WORTH] it.\nSERVICE The<SERVICE ENTITY >is [ALWAYS FRIENDLY] and fast .\nSTAFF <STAFF ENTITY >was [EXTREMELY HELPFUL] and knowledgeable and was on top of everything.\nNegative\nRESTAURANT I was appalled by the experience and will [NOT FREQUENT] this <RESTAURANT ENTITY >ever again.\nCUISINE [ITS YOUR] typical <CUISINE ENTITY >buffet , nothing to rave about .\nFOOD <FOOD ENTITY >smelled [VERY BAD] and tasted worse .\nSERVICE We waited another 5 minutes , [STILL NO] <SERVICE ENTITY >.\nSTAFF I went with 5 friends and our <STAFF ENTITY >was [REALLY RUDE] .\nTable 6: Examples of Learned Creative Sentence Templates by Entity and Polarity\nBASIC The bar is beautiful. For example, sample slot values could\nbe:fRESTAURANT [BAR],CUISINE [JAPANESE ],\nFOOD [UDON ],SERVICE [CUSTOMER SERVICE ],\nSTAFF [HOSTS ]g.\nOur objective is to evaluate whether we can\nimprove upon vanilla-style hand-crafted templates\nfor restaurant reviews by utilizing in hyperbolic\nand creative elements of organic reviews that we\nharvest. We set up an annotation experiment on\nAmazon Mechanical Turk4, where each Human\nIntelligence Task (HIT) presents Turkers with a\n4https://www.mturk.com/sample of our three review variations, all of the\nsame polarity and instantiated with the same enti-\nties. Turkers are asked to judge the reviews based\non three criteria: convincingness (Do you believe\nthe opinion given?), interestingness (Is the review\nengaging?), and naturalness (Is the review coher-\nent?). Turkers are asked to rate each review on\na three point scale ( high, medium and low) for\neach criteria. We release 200 variation triples (100\nper polarity class) and ask for ﬁve judgements per\nHIT, tagging a review with a quality if the majority\nof annotators agree on it (i.e. 3 or more Turkers).\nAverage agreement for individual Turkers with the\nmajority is above 73%.\nFigure 1 shows the distribution of high,\nmedium, andlowscores for each of the variation\ntypes for each criterion. From the results, we ob-\nserve that for all criteria, the CREATIVE class has\nthe highest distribution of high majority votes. In-\nterestingly, although we hypothesized that the HY-\nPERBOLIC reviews would be better received than\nthe BASIC reviews, we observe that in fact the\nBASIC reviews receive more high votes on con-\nvincingness . We note that for the future ranking,\nmore context information is necessary when se-\nlecting appropriate hyperbolic patterns with which\nto modify the BASIC reviews. For example, if a\nlearned pattern is OTHER AMAZING , the pattern\nshould only be used when a set of items are being\ndescribed, and not stand-alone. Similarly, the BA-\nSICreviews are also more natural than the HYPER -\nBOLIC ones, although both variation types score\nvery similar percentages for medium scores.\nFor the creative reviews, a crucial next step\nfor ranking is to consider context and develop\nheuristics for ﬁnding the most appropriate enti-\nties for lexicalization. For example, for very\nspeciﬁc creative templates such as: “I also got\none that HAD NOT been separated , so it was\n[JUST HALF] of a <FOOD ENTITY >. ”, or\n“The <FOOD ENTITY >were similarly a mix\nof nearly raw to overly crisp. ” , it is necessary to\nselect food items similar to the original instantia-\ntions, or to characterize and classify entities based\non speciﬁc properties.\nFigure 1: Distribution of Template Variations by\nEvaluation Criteria\nGiven the high appeal of the CREATIVE reviews\non all counts, we are interested in more closely\nexploring examples in the data. Table 8 shows\ntwo examples of CREATIVE reviews: one that re-\nceived high scores on all criteria, and one that re-\nceived majority (no creative review received all\nlows). It is clear that the biggest disconnect in\nthe low-scoring creative review is the coherence\nbetween sentences, which as an important next\nstep to consider as future work given the proof-\nof-concept presented here. We also note that we\ncan also improve the fully high-scoring review by\nﬁxing grammatical errors and applying more in-\nformed content selection.\nTo get a better sense of how grammatically cor-\nrect the review template variations are, we conduct\nanother evaluation study where we present Turk-\ners with the same set of reviews, and ask them\nto rate each review based on the content (check-\ning subject-verb agreement, plurality, tense, etc.).\nSimilar to the previous study, we gather 5 judge-\nments for each set of three variations, and aggre-\ngate results using majority vote. Average agree-\nment for individual Turkers with the majority in\nthis task is above 80%, higher than the more sub-ALL\nHIGHIt is one of my favorite cafe in las vegas. We ﬁnd\nthat for all three variations, the med class receives\nthe majority of the votes, but that the BASIC re-\nviews are the most grammatically correct (since\nthe templates are designed, not harvested). Sim-\nilarly, the HYPERBOLIC reviews have the largest\npercentage of lowscores, since their creation in-\nvolves modifying templates with learned adjec-\ntives. Ranking the best patterns/sentences to use\nwill allow us to improve the grammatical coher-\nence of the templatized utterances for the HYPER -\nBOLIC and CREATIVE classes.\nTo better understand the linguistic characteris-\ntics of the creative reviews by class, we run the\nLinguistic Inquiry and Word Count (LIWC) tool\n(Pennebaker et al., 2015) on the full set of 100\nPOSITIVE and 100 NEGATIVE creative reviews.\nWhen comparing the linguistic categories for each\nclass, we ﬁnd that the difference between the POS-\nITIVE and NEGATIVE reviews are signiﬁcant ( p <\n0:05, t-test) for many of the categories. Table 9\nshows some of the most interesting categories5.\nOn average, the POSITIVE templates are char-\n5All of the categories are statistically signiﬁcant, and are\nshown in order of most to least signiﬁcant.\nFigure 2: Distribution of V otes on Template Vari-\nation Grammar\nPositive Negative\nAFFECTIVE PROC.\nEXCLAMATIONS\nFRIENDS\n1stPERSON SINGULAR\nACHIEVE\nCERTAINTY\nBIOLOGICAL PROC.\nINGESTIONS\nINSIGHT\nREWARDDIFFERENTIATION\nRISK\n1stPERSON PLURAL\nANXIETY\nADVERBS\nANGER\nSOCIAL PROC.\n2ndPERSON\nMOTION\nCOGNITIVE PROC.\nTable 9: Statistically Signiﬁcant LIWC Categories\nby Polarity\nacterized by word classes that exemplify achieve-\nment (e.g. “even better”, “champion” ) and cer-\ntainty (e.g. “always excellent”, “absolutely amaz-\ning”, and“deﬁnitely my go-to place” ). As well as\n1stperson statements relating to use of the senses\n(affective processes like “my favorite place to get\nrice in Las Vegas!” , biological processes ( “I just\nhad the most amazingly delicious and freshly pre-\npared couscous!” ), and ingestion ( “good, tasty\ncomfort pizza” ).\nThe negative contains more oppositional lan-\nguage directed at the second person, often as ad-\nvice ( “you can get a much better pizza elsewhere\nat far less cost. ” ), with categories like differentia-\ntion ( “but it’s not great” ), and strong emotion in-\ndicators like anxiety ( “horrible service, ﬁnally just\nleft”) and anger ( “I was so angry that I contacted\nthe restaurant manager” ).\n6 Conclusions\nIn this paper, we show that we can construct con-\nvincing, interesting, and natural restaurant review\ntemplates by using a data-driven method to har-\nvest highly descriptive sentences from hyperbolic\nrestaurant reviews. We generate three variations\nof review templates, ranging from very basic, to\nhyperbolic, to very creative, and show that the cre-\native ones are more appealing to readers than the\nothers. Future work will focus on ranking the can-\ndidate sentence templates we harvest to improve\nreview coherence. As we develop better templates,\nwe will evaluate them against baselines from ex-\nisting NLG systems to guide our generation of\nmore exciting and expressive stylistically varied\nreviews.References\nSrinivas Bangalore and Owen Rambow. 2000. {"id": "1811.08495v1", "paper_content": "Are pre-trained CNNs good feature extractors for\nanomaly detection in surveillance videos?\nTiago S. Nazare\u0003z, Rodrigo F. de Mello\u0003, Moacir A. Ponti\u0003\n\u0003ICMC, University of São Paulo, São Carlos, Brazil.\nzData Science Team, Itaú-Unibanco, São Paulo, Brazil.\nEmail: tiagosn@usp.br, mello@icmc.usp.br, moacir@icmc.usp.br\nAbstract —Recently, several techniques have been explored to\ndetect unusual behaviour in surveillance videos. Nevertheless,\nfew studies leverage features from pre-trained CNNs and none\nof then present a comparison of features generate by different\nmodels. Motivated by this gap, we compare features extracted\nby four state-of-the-art image classiﬁcation networks as a way\nof describing patches from security video frames. We carry out\nexperiments on the Ped1 and Ped2 datasets and analyze the usage\nof different feature normalization techniques. Our results indicate\nthat choosing the appropriate normalization is crucial to improve\nthe anomaly detection performance when working with CNN\nfeatures. Also, in the Ped2 dataset our approach was able to\nobtain results comparable to the ones of several state-of-the-art\nmethods. Lastly, as our method only considers the appearance of\neach frame, we believe that it can be combined with approaches\nthat focus on motion patterns to further improve performance.\nI. I NTRODUCTION\nNowadays security cameras are widely employed to monitor\npublic spaces – such as malls, squares and universities. Yet,\nthose surveillance cameras may be ineffective, mainly because\neach video feed needs a person constantly watching it. Keeping\ntrack of the events in security videos is very hard for humans,\nespecially for two reasons: 1) a single person is responsible for\nmonitoring several cameras simultaneously [1]; 2) it is hard\nfor people to maintain an acceptable level of attention when\nwatching this kind of video [2]. Due to the aforementioned\nissues, security footage is of little help with regards to pre-\nventing dangerous situations and end up being used mostly for\ninvestigation purposes, after something already happened.\nThe ineffectuality of surveillance systems motivated the\nmachine vision community to work on automated systems to\ndetect unusual behaviour in security videos, consequently, we\nhave seen outstanding advances in this area. Over the last\nfew years a great deal of methods have been proposed to\ndetect anomalies in videos. Among the approaches employed\nto tackle this issue we have: time series decomposition of\noptical-ﬂow [3], optical-ﬂow features [4], [5], dictionary learn-\ning [6], auto-encoders [7] and GANs (generative adversarial\nnetworks) [8]. Despite all progress obtained over the last\nfew years, automatically detecting unusual events in videos\nremains an open research area and several approaches are yet\nto be explored.\nAny opinions, ﬁndings, and conclusions expressed in this manuscript are\nthose of the authors and do not necessarily reﬂect the views, ofﬁcial policy\nor position of the Itaú-Unibanco, FAPESP and CNPq.One kind of approach that – to the best of our knowl-\nedge – have not yet been broadly investigated in anomaly\ndetection scenarios is transfer-learning. This technique consists\nof leveraging knowledge obtained from solving a particular\nproblem to solve a different one (on a related domain). With\nregards to CNNs most transfer learning applications use pre-\ntrained networks as feature extractors or as starting point for\ntraining. In recent years, such usage of pre-trained networks\nhas been considered to be very effective by various studies [9],\neven when the original and target domains are considerably\ndifferent [10]. Still, such performance is not guaranteed for\nevery scenario, as pointed out by some other studies [11],\n[12].\nMotivated by the aforementioned results and the lack of\ninvestigation regarding the usage of pre-trained CNNs to detect\nunusual behaviour in security videos, in this paper, we devote\nour efforts to evaluate the application of several state-of-the-art\nimage classiﬁcation CNNs as features extractor machines for\nsurveillance footage. We compared features generated by four\nnetworks (VGG-16 [13], RestNet-50 [14], Xception [15] and\nDenseNet-121 [16]) as a way to describe appearance for frame\nregions of security videos. Despite neglecting the motion part\nof anomaly detection in videos, our experiments (conducted\nusing the Ped1 and Ped2 UCSD datasets [17], [18]) show that\nthose features are able to achieve competitive results.\nII. R ELATED WORK\nRecently, the usage of CNN features has been explored\nto detect unusual activities in surveillance footage. In [19],\nthe authors describe image regions using AlexNet [20] and,\nthen, track variations in such description in order to detect\nanomalies. With this tracking, they are able to use a image\nbased CNN to ﬁnd both motion and appearance anomalies.\nIn [21] a pre-trained C3D model [22] was employed for\nfeature extraction. Such model was originally designed for\naction recognition in videos, thus it learns spatio-temporal\nrepresentations, which is very useful to deal with both motion\nand appearance abnormalities. Nevertheless, in [21] the pre-\ntrained model is used in a classiﬁcation setup, therefore it has\naccess to instances of both normal and anomalous behaviour\nduring training.\nIn spite of the fact that are some studies where pre-trained\nnetworks were employed to detect anomalies in security\nvideos, they do not compare several models in order toarXiv:1811.08495v1  [cs.CV]  20 Nov 2018\nCNN \nfeature \nextractor \nTest\nSetScaler IPCA Training \nSetTraining \nSet Prep. \nTest\nSet Prep. data prep data prep \ntraining 1-NN \n(FLANN) \nResults \nTraining videos \nTest videos \nstep 1: feature extraction step 2: data preparation step 3: model training and evaluation Fig. 1: Experimental setup diagram.\ndetermine which one is best suited for the application. For\nthis reason, in this paper we design a experimental setup and\npresent several experimental results — employing different\nnetwork architectures and data normalization procedures — in\norder to shed some light on this issue. Instead of attempting\nto outperform state-of-the-art methods, we aim at a better\nunderstanding of how off-the-shelf CNN features behave for\nthe application of video surveillance, providing a guideline for\nfuture research on how to choose the appropriate model for\nthis task.\nIII. E XPERIMENTAL SETUP\nIn order to measure how well the features extracted from\npre-trained image classiﬁcation CNNs perform, when de-\ntecting anomalies in surveillance videos, we employed the\nexperimental setup depicted in Figure 1. This experimental\nsetup has three steps.\nIn the ﬁrst step of our setup we start by converting\neach video frame to 384\u0002256 pixels. Next, we take image\nregions of 32\u000232pixels using a stride of 16 pixels and\nperform a forward pass of each of these patches through the\nconvolutional part of a CNN pre-trained on the ImageNet\ndataset [23]. This image patch end up being represented by\ndfeatures, where the value of ddepends on the network\n– it is equal to the number of ﬁlters (neurons) at the last\nconvolutional layer of the network. At this point one can notice\nthat using the convolutional part of the network – instead of\nthe entire network – is rather convenient in our framework.\nThis is due to the fact that when using the entire networks\nwe have to provide input images of the same size of the\noriginal dataset (e.g. 224\u0002224), while with the convolutional\npart of the network we do not have such constraint. In this\npart of our setup, we investigated features generated by the\nfollowing state-of-the-art image classiﬁcation models (trained\non ImageNet): VGG-16 [13], ResNet-50 [14], Xception [15]\nand DenseNet-121 [16]. Please refer to Section III-B for more\ndetails on each model and also for the number of features\ngenerated by each one of them.In the second step we prepared the data obtained on the\nﬁrst step in order to use it with an anomaly detection method.\nTo do so, we started by normalizing the data, in this part\nwe tested the z-score, 0-1, L1 and L2 normalization methods\n(see Section III-A for a description of these techniques).\nGiven that we employed the nearest neighbour technique to\ndetect anomalies, this normalization step is fundamental to\nthe performance of the system. After normalizing the data we\nused the Incremental PCA (IPCA) algorithm [24] to reduce\ndata dimensionality to 50 and 100 dimensions. This particular\nmethod was chosen for two reasons: 1) it can be used to\nreduce the number of features and, consequently, speed up\nthe computation of nearest neighbours; 2) it does not need\nto load the entire dataset into RAM in order to compute its\ntransformation, so it can deal with large datasets such as those\nused in this paper.\nLastly, in the third step of our experimental setup, we train\nan 1-NN model using the datasets obtained in step two (with\n50 or 100 features depending on the experiment). Then, we\nﬁnd the distance of all the test set instances to their nearest\nneighbour in the training set. Those distances are used as\nan anomaly score. Hence, the higher the distance, the more\na instance (image region) is considered to be anomalous.\nIt is important to notice that we train only one model to\nwork with descriptions from the entire image. Also, aiming\nto speed up our anomaly detection framework, we used the\napproximate nearest neighbour method from [25]. Next, we\nobtain the anomaly score of a frame simply by taking the\nmax score among its patches. Based on those frame scores\nwe can compute the AUC (Area Under ROC Curve) and EER\n(Equal Error Ratio) on a frame-level classiﬁcation.\nOur experiments were carried out on the UCSD video\nanomaly detection datasets: Ped1 and Ped2 [17], [18]. More\ninformation about the two datasets are presented Section III-C.\nA. Data normalization\nIn our experiments we used the following four data normal-\nization methods:\n\u000fz-score : outputs features centered at the origin (i.e. with\nzero mean) and with unit standard deviation by comput-\ning:\nfi=fi\u0000mean (f)\nstd(f);\nwhere fiis the value of feature ffor instance i, and the\nmean and standard deviation of fis obtained using the\nentire training set;\n\u000f0-1: outputs features in the [0;1]interval. To do so, it\nuses the following formula:\nfi=fi\u0000min(f)\nmax(f)\u0000min(f);\nwhere fiis the value of feature ffor instance iand the\nmax and min of fare obtained from the training set;\n\u000fL1: in this method all instances (dataset rows) are scaled\nso their L1 norm are equal to one, as follows:\nx=xP\nfjxfj;\nwhere xis an instance and jxfjis the absolute value of\nthef-thfeature of x.\n\u000fL2: similar to the previous one, but using the L2 norm\ninstead, as in the equation bellow:\nx=xqP\nfx2\nf:\nB. CNN models\nBellow, we quickly present some of the main characteristics\nof the four CNNs used as feature extractors in our experiments.\nVGG-16 [13]: is composed of 16 layers, 13 of them con-\nvolutional and the remaining 3 dense layers. All convolutional\nlayers use 3\u00023kernels and ReLU as activation function. Also,\nthose 13 layers are divided into 6 groups and each group has\na max-pooling layer at its end.\nResNet-50 [14]: uses an alternative layer conﬁguration\nmethod, called residual units. Residual units – such as the\none depicted in Figure 2 – allow deeper models to be learned\nby using shortcut connections to avoid vanishing/exploding\ngradients during training. This network has 49 convolutional\nlayers and only one fully connected layer. All but one of its\nconvolutional layers are organized into 16 residual units like\nthe one of Figure 2.\nXception [15]: is inspired by Inception V3 [26], and built\nby replacing the inception modules with depthwise separable\nconvolutions. This type of convolution is performed by, ﬁrst,\napplying convolutions (e.g. using 3\u00023kernels) to each tensor\nchannel separately and, then, applying an 1\u00021convolution\nto all channels, this process is illustrated in Figure 3. By\nusing this type of operation the Xception model was able to\noutperform Inception V3 for image classiﬁcation using the\nsame number of trainable parameters.\nConv2D: 1 ✕1 filters \nConv2D: 3 ✕3 filters \nConv2D: 1 ✕1 filters Fig. 2: Residual unit employed by ResNet-50 [14].\nFig. 3: Depthwise separable convolution layer used by Xcep-\ntion [15].\nDenseNet-121 [16]: is an architecture similar to ResNets\nbecause they also use shortcut/skipping connections. Never-\ntheless, instead of employing this type of connection in some\nspeciﬁc parts of the network, they use shortcut connections to\nconnect every layer to all of its subsequent layers. The authors\nclaim this approach avoids vanishing/exploding gradients and\nallows the training of even deeper networks when compared\nto ResNets.\nAs previously mentioned, those models have different num-\nber of trainable parameters and generate descriptors of dif-\nferent sizes. In Table I, we present the number of trainable\nparameters in the convolutional part (feature extractor part)\nand the number of generated features of each CNN used in\nour experiments.\nTABLE I: Number of trainable parameters and output features\nfor each one of the network architectures used in our experi-\nments.\nfeature extractor # of trainable parameters # of features\nVGG-16 [13] 14,714,688 512\nResNet-50 [14] 23,534,592 2048\nXception [15] 20,806,952 2048\nDenseNet-121 [16] 6,953,856 1024\nC. Datasets\nBoth datasets employed in our experiments were obtained\nfor stationary surveillance cameras at the UCSD (University of\nCalifornia, San Diego) campus [17], [18]. This means that all\nof their videos are real (they were not staged). Each dataset\ncontains videos from a single camera and only the test set\nhas anomalous events. Some speciﬁcations and sample images\nfrom both datasets are presented in Table II and Figure 4,\nrespectively.\nFig. 4: Frames from Ped1 (ﬁrst row) and Ped2 (second row), with anomalies manually highlighted by red boxes.\nTABLE II: Video speciﬁcations for Ped1 and Ped2.\ncharacteristicdataset\nPed1 Ped2\ntraining videos 34 16\ntest videos 36 12\nframe resolution 238\u0002158 360\u0002240\nfps 10 10\nframes per video 200 120\u0000180\nD. Reproducibility remarks\nIn our experiments we used the Keras [27] library, and\nits pre-trained models, for feature extraction. In order to\nnormalize our features and compute IPCA, we used the\nScikit-learn [28] library. Lastly, for the approximate\nnearest neighbour distance computation we have used the\nFLANN [29] library. To facilitate the reproduction of our\nresults our source code is publicly available1.\nIV. R ESULTS AND DISCUSSION\nAs stated in Section III, we trained nearest neighbour\nmodels using features extracted from four pre-trained CNNs.\nIn those tests, before training our nearest neighbour models,\nwe ﬁrst normalized our features and, then, used IPCA to\nreduce the number of dimensions. Aiming at obtaining the\nbest results out of our features we experimented with several\nconﬁguration regarding both the normalization technique and\nthe number of dimensions used after the IPCA transformation.\nThe results of such experiments are presented in Table III.\nBy looking at those results it is possible to notice that the\nnormalization method can greatly impact anomaly detection\n1Our source code is available at https://github.com/tiagosn/cnn_features_\nanomaly_detection.results. Also, it is possible to realize that not all networks\nhave the same behaviour with regards to normalization. With\nResNet-50 and Xcpetion features we usually obtained the\nbest results by using the z-score normalization, while with\nDenseNet-121 the best results were obtained by using the 0-1\nnormalization. Considering the number of dimensions used to\ntrain the model, in most cases, going from 50 to 100 features\nhas shown to be beneﬁcial. Nevertheless, it is important to\nkeep in mind that increasing the number of features makes\nthe nearest neighbour inference slower.\nNow, we compare the best results obtained in our test\nagainst classic and state-of-the-art results methods on the\nUCSD (Ped1 and Ped2) datasets. Such comparison is pre-\nsented in Table IV. Although the CNN features are not better\nwhen compared to state-of-the-art results, the performance was\nreasonable. Specially, considering that feature extraction was\nnot trained with any data from the target task (i.e. Ped1 and\nPed2 frames) and motion information was ignored when we\nonly described individual frames. In particular for Ped2, the\nresults are comparable to the ones obtained by some state-of-\nthe-art methods, therefore they can be considered as a good\nbaseline for this dataset. In the case of Ped1, the results are\nonly comparable to some classic methods. We believe that\none of the main reasons for these lower performance is the\nfact that Ped1 has changes in perspective – objects change\nsize (regarding number of pixels) according to the image\nregion that they are in. Such characteristic can hamper the\nperformance in a setup like ours, where a single model is\ntrained to deal with image patches from the entire frame.\nV. C ONCLUSION\nIn this paper we investigated the usage of pre-trained image\nclassiﬁcation CNNs as feature extractors to tackle the detection\nTABLE III: Frame level detection results on Ped1 and Ped2 datasets using several pre-trained CNNs as feature extractors,\nvarious feature normalization techniques and approximate nearest neighbour. According to our experiments\nsuch networks – when paired with suitable data normalization\ntechniques – can be very useful to detect abnormal events in\nsecurity videos. This can be noticed in the Ped2 experiments,where they were able to achieve results comparable to the\nones of the state-of-the-art techniques. Another important fact\nregarding our experiments is that we only explored those\nfeatures to model appearance anomalies; hence, we neglected\nthe motion part of the anomalies. That being said, we strongly\nbelieve that the method proposed in this work can be combined\nwith methods that focus on motion anomalies (e.g. [4], [3])\nto obtain better results.\nVI. F UTURE WORK\nThe main points that we intent to investigate in feature\nresearch are:\n\u000fThe performance of such features in RGB surveillance\nvideos like the ones of the Avenue dataset [34]. Such\nanalysis can help to better understand if gray scale image\ncan hamper the description capabilities of CNNs trained\nin RGB domains;\n\u000fCombine our method with some methods that only tackle\nmotion related anomalies, such as the ones presented\nin [4], [3], to see if their fusion can further improve\nperformance;\n\u000fTest the usage of other anomaly detection techniques, for\ninstance Isolation Forest [37] with those CNN features.\nACKNOWLEDGMENT\nThis work was supported by FAPESP (grants #2015/04883-\n0 and #2016/16111-4), CNPq (grant #307973/2017-4),\nItaú-Unibanco and partially supported by CEPID-CeMEAI\n(FAPESP grant #2013/07375-0).\nREFERENCES\n[1] H. {"id": "1811.03736v1", "paper_content": "1\nSemantic and Contrast-Aware Saliency\nXiaoshuai Sun\nAbstract —In this paper, we proposed an integrated model\nof semantic-aware and contrast-aware saliency combining both\nbottom-up and top-down cues for effective saliency estimation\nand eye ﬁxation prediction. The proposed model processes visual\ninformation using two pathways. The ﬁrst pathway aims to\ncapture the attractive semantic information in images, especially\nfor the presence of meaningful objects and object parts such as\nhuman faces. The second pathway is based on multi-scale on-line\nfeature learning and information maximization, which learns an\nadaptive sparse representation for the input and discovers the\nhigh contrast salient patterns within the image context. The two\npathways characterize both long-term and short-term attention\ncues and are integrated dynamically using maxima normaliza-\ntion. We investigate two different implementations of the semantic\npathway including an End-to-End deep neural network solution\nand a dynamic feature integration solution, resulting in the\nSCA and SCAFI model respectively. Experimental results on\nartiﬁcial images and 5 popular benchmark datasets demonstrate\nthe superior performance and better plausibility of the proposed\nmodel over both classic approaches and recent deep models.\nIndex Terms —Saliency, feature integration, eye ﬁxation pre-\ndiction.\nI. I NTRODUCTION\nTHE last two decades have witnessed enormous devel-\nopment in the ﬁeld of computational visual attention\nmodeling and saliency detection [1]. Various models, datasets,\nand evaluation metrics are proposed to help machines better\nunderstand and predict human viewing behavior. By pro-\nducing a 2D/3D saliency map that predicts where human\nlook, a computational model can be applied to many low-\nlevel computer vision applications, e.g. image retargeting [2],\nvideo compression [3], [4], detecting abnormal patterns [5],\nsegmenting proto objects [6], generating object proposals [7].\nThe concept of “saliency” were investigated not only in\nearly vision modeling but also in many engineering applica-\ntions such as image compression [10], object recognition [11]\nand tracking [12], robot navigation [13], design and advertising\n[14] etc. In the early stage, the research works of attention and\nsaliency are mostly motivated by biological priors [5], [15],\nstatistical assumptions [16] frequency domain characteristics\n[6], [17] and information theory [18], [19]. Some papers also\ndraw their inspirations by analyzing the statistical property of\nground-truth eye-tracking data [20], [21], [22].\nDriven by the great success of deep neural networks, the\ndeep learning scheme were recently introduced to saliency\nresearch and undoubtfully achieved ground-break success in\npredicting human eye-ﬁxations [23], [24], [8]. The remarkable\nThis work was supported in part by the National Natural Science Foundation\nof China under Project 61472103, 61572108 and 61632007, and in part by\nthe Australian Research Council under Grant FT130101530.\nXiaoshuai Sun was with the School of Computer Science and\nTechnology, Harbin Institute of Technology, Harbin, China, email:\nfxiaoshuaisung@hit.edu.cn.\nInput DPN (2016)                             DeepGazeII (2016)                         SCAFI(Ours)       \nFig. 1. Illustration of our model. For each row, we show the input image (left),\nthe saliency prediction result of DPN [8],DeepGazeII [9] and our proposed\nSCAFI model. The red dot in each saliency map indicates the location of the\nmaximum value. Compared to traditional deep models, our method highlights\nboth the semantic-aware and contrast-aware saliency.\nperformance of deep models relies very much on their ability\nof characterizing semantic information in the inputs. However,\nthey can hardly explain the psychophysical evidence, and often\nproduce unreasonable responses to obvious salient patterns.\nFor instance, in Figure 1, the saliency maps produced by\nDPN [8] and DeepGazeII [9] only characterize the presence\nof meaningful objects, e.g. faces and ﬁsh, but fail to localize\nthe most salient pattern in the image.\nAs discussed in [25], some eye ﬁxations are directed at\nobjects, while others are attracted by local feature contrast\nwhich is relatively detached from semantics. It’s crucial to\nconsider both semantic and contrast cues for the construction\nof a plausible and effective saliency model. On one hand,\nthe integration of deep neural network is inevitable, since\ntraditional methods can not discover or make effective use of\nlarge-scale features that represent attractive semantic objects\nor common interests. On the other hand, we must carefully\nformulate the model to ensure it is truely a model of saliency\ninstead of a simpliﬁed object detector derived from recog-\nnition models. Based on the above considerations, we have\nproposed an integrated saliency model, namely SCA [26],\nwhich combines both semantic and contrast cues for effective\nsaliency estimation and eye-ﬁxation prediction. SCA contains\ntwo pathways and responses to both bottom-up and top-down\ncues. In the experiments, we demonstrated that our SCA model\nsteadily outperforms both the traditional saliency models and\nthe cutting-edge End-To-End deep models. Despite its superior\nperformance, SCA still has its own limitations:\n1) It only inherits the initial convolutional layers of the\nVGG model which might cause semantic loss during the\ngeneration of semantic-aware saliency;\n2) It requires large-scale eye-ﬁxation data for the training\nof the semantic pathway.\nIn this paper, to overcome the limitations of SCA , we\nproposed a new model called SCAFI which predicts saliencyarXiv:1811.03736v1  [cs.CV]  9 Nov 2018\n2\nbased on semantic and contrast-aware feature integration.\nSCAFI is a heuristic model which shares the same inspiration\nofSCA , but achieves much better performance by directly\npooling the semantic information out of the VGG net. Com-\npared to traditional deep model (Figure 1), SCAFI is learning-\nfree and highlights both the semantic interests and the bottom-\nup contrast. The main contributions of our work are listed as\nfollows:\n\u000fWe propose an heuristic saliency model ( SCAFI ) that\ncharacterizes both top-down and bottom-up saliency cues\nin a uniﬁed framework.\n\u000fCompared to our previous model SCA ,SCAFI directly\nextracts the semantic information from the VGG net,\nwhich avoids the ﬁne-tuning process and thus does not\nrequire additional labeled data to perform the eye-ﬁxation\nprediction task.\n\u000fThe SCAFI model outperforms the state-of-art methods,\nincluding recent deep models (e.g. DPN , winner of LSUN\nChallenge 2015) and our previous model SCA , on all 5\neye-ﬁxation datasets.\n\u000fDespite its superior performance in eye ﬁxation pre-\ndiction task, the SCAFI model also produces plausible\nresponses to images with pre-attentive patterns and high-\ncontrast objects.\nThe following parts of the paper are organized as follows.\nSec. II introduces the most related works in detail. Sec. III\npresents our SCA andSCAFI model and some intermediate\nresults. In Sec.IV, we show the main experimental results\non several well-acknowledged datasets and provided more\ncomparisons with different parameter settings. Finally, we\nconclude our work in Sec.V.\nII. R ELATED WORKS\nThere are many excellent research works contributed to this\ntopic, most of them can be found in recent surveys [1], [27].\nSaliency models can be classiﬁed into two categories: ones\ndriven by the task of salient object segmentation , and the\nothers driven by the task of human eye-ﬁxation prediction .\nThe two tasks have their own benchmark datasets and pre-\nferred evaluation metrics. Our paper focuses on the ﬁxation\nprediction task, thus we didnt compared to the segmentation\nmethods such as RFCNs [28], DHSNet [29], DISC [30], and\ndidnt use PR-curves and F-measure metrics for the evaluation.\nInstead, we follow the work of Bruce et al. [25] and Lebor ´an\net al . [31], and use the ﬁxation datasets and shufﬂed AUC\nmetric for the evaluation. In this section, we mainly introduce\nthe related works that focus on eye-ﬁxation prediction task\nincluding the most related traditional models, along with some\nrecently proposed deep-learning models.\nA. Traditional Heuristic Saliency Model\nIttiet al. [5] implemented the very ﬁrst computational model\n(ITTI ) that generates 2D saliency map based on the Feature\nIntegration theory [32]. The center-surround operation inspires\nmany subsequent models [16]. Graph-based Visual Saliency\n(GBVS ) model [33] adopts the same bio-inspired features in[5] yet a different parallelized graph computation measurement\nto compute visual saliency.\nBased on sparse representation, Bruce and Tsotsos [18] pro-\nposed the AIM model (Attention by Information Maximiza-\ntion) which adopts the self-information of ICA coefﬁcients as\nthe measure for signal saliency. Also based on information\ntheory and sparse coding, Hou and Zhang [19] proposed the\ndynamic visual attention ( DV A ) model which deﬁnes spatio-\ntemporal saliency as incremental coding length. Garcia et al.\n[22], [31] proposed the A WS (Adaptive Whitening Saliency)\nmodel which relies on a contextually adapted representation\nproduced through adaptive whitening of color and scale fea-\ntures. Inspired by the success of online sparse representation,\nwe also present a uniﬁed statistical framework named SGP\n[20], [21] (Super-Gaussian Component Pursuit) which exploits\nstatistical priors for the joint modeling of both saccadic eye\nmovements and visual saliency.\nHou et al. [6] proposed a highly efﬁcient saliency de-\ntection algorithm by exploring the spectral residual ( SR) in\nthe frequency domain. SRhighlights the salient regions by\nmanipulating the amplitude spectrum of the images’ Fourier\ntransformation. Inspired by [6], Guo et al. [34] achieved\nfast and robust spatio-temporal saliency detection by using\nthe phase spectrum of Quaternion Fourier Transform. As\na theoretical revision of SR, [17] proposed a new visual\ndescriptor named Image Signature ( SIG) based on the Discrete\nFourier Transform of images, which was proved to be more\neffective in explaining saccadic eye movements and change\nblindness of human vision.\nDespite the above models, there are many other insight-\nful works that detect visual saliency using different types\nof measures, e.g. Bayesian Surprise [35], Center-Surround\nDiscriminant Power [36], Short-Term Self-Information [37],\nSpatially Weighted Dissimilarity [38], Site Entropy Rate [39],\nRarity [40] and Self-Resemblance [41].\nB. Learning-based Saliency Model via Deep Neural Network\nProvided with enough training data, deep model can achieve\nground-breaking performance that are far better than tra-\nditional methods, sometime even outperform humans. The\nensembles of Deep Networks ( eDN ) [42] is the ﬁrst attemp\nat modeling saiency with deep models, which combines three\ndifferent convnet layers using a linear classifer. Different from\neDN , recent models such as DeepGaze [23], SALICON [24]\nandFOCUS [25] integrate pre-trained layers from large-scale\nCNN models. Especially, SALICON [24], DeepFix [43] and\nDPN [8] use the VGG network pre-trained on ImageNet to\ninitialize their convolutional layers and then train the rest\nlayers based on ground-truth saliency maps generated using\nhuman eye-ﬁxation data.\nBeneﬁt from the powerful visual representation embedded\ninVGG net, the above models signiﬁcantly outperform tra-\nditional methods in eye-ﬁxation prediction task on almost\nall benchmark datasets. In this paper, we mainly compared\nour model with SALICON [24], DeepGazeII [9] and DPN [8].\n3\nSALICON1and DeepGazeII2provide on-line Web service\nto receive image submissions and generate saliency maps.\nDPN3is a fully open source End-to-End model with good\ncomputation efﬁciency and state-of-the-art performance.\nC. Relationship between Previous Methods and Ours\nThe success of the above-mentioned deep models is un-\ndoubtfully remarkable. The ground-breaking improvements\nachieved by integrating the pre-trained convolutional layers\ndirectly motivates our SCA model. However, the ﬁne-tuning\nof the deep network is not an easy task, and most of the current\nmodels can not reliably detect and pop out high-contrast signal\nin the input context, even with a large-scale training set such\nasSALICON [44]. In contrast, traditional adaptive methods\nsuch as A WS [22] and SGP [21] go through a pure bottom-\nup pathway and are hardly affected by the dense presence\nof semantics. This further inspires us to formulate our SCAFI\nmodel which handles top-down semantic cues and the bottom-\nup contrast in two independent pathways.\nIII. T HEPROPOSED MODEL\nIn this section, we ﬁrst formulate our problem and then\nintroduce our integrated saliency model SCAFI along with its\nalternative version SCA . We also present the implementation\ndetails and the intermediate results of the proposed models.\nA. Problem Deﬁnation\nLetIbe an image, Yeyebe a set of pixel locations recording\nhuman eye ﬁxations, and Yrnda set of random or pseudo-\nrandom pixel locations. The task of eye ﬁxation prediction is\nto generate a saliency map Swhich predicts the probability of\nevery pixel in the image for being a true human eye ﬁxation.\nEye-ﬁxation prediction is initially proposed for the evaluation\nof computational visual attention models. Since visual atten-\ntion can be driven by both bottom-up and top-down cues [1],\nsaliency detection methods can also be divided into two main\ncategories: 1. Unsupervised methods constructed based on\nbiological and psychological knowledge of the human vision\nsystem; 2. Supervised methods which adopt machine learning\ntechniques to train a saliency model based on visual datasets\nwith eye tracking labels.\n1) Saliency Detection as a Bottom-up Problem: Salient\nstimuli often have the capability of attracting visual attention\ntoward their locations in a pure bottom-up manner. Such kind\nof saliency is a natural property of the presented stimuli,\nwhich is independent of speciﬁc tasks or goals and can be\ndetected by a ﬁxed computational model. Bottom-up saliency\ndetection approaches attract a lot of attention between the\n1980s and 2010s. On one hand, they are theoretically trustful\nsince most of them are the computational implementation of\nsome well-studied psychological theories and hypotheses, e.g.\nFeature Integration [32] and Information Maximization [18].\nOn the other hand, these models indeed generate reasonable\n1SALICON : http://salicon.net/demo/#\n2DeepGazeII : https://deepgaze.bethgelab.org/\n3DPN : https://github.com/imatge-upc/saliency-2016-cvprresponses to various controlled stimuli compared with those\nfrom human subjects. However, bottom-up methods can hardly\nmodel the effect of semantic factors in natural scenes such\nas the presence of human faces or other meaningful objects\nwith complex appearance. Judd et al. [46] studied the human\ngaze patterns over thousands of images and shows that people\nspontaneously focus their attention on image areas with certain\nobjects. Such observation indicates that top-down inﬂuences\nexist as a long-term supervision and might affect human\nviewing behavior with or without explicit task instructions.\n2) Saliency Detection as a Top-down Problem: To fully\nmodel the top-down inﬂuences, recent eye-ﬁxation prediction\nmethods are mostly based on supervised or semi-supervised\nmachine learning techniques. Typically, a saliency model is\nobtained by training a pixel-wise eye-ﬁxation predictor, which\ncan be formulated as the following optimization problem given\nIandY=Yeye[Yrndwith Euclidean Loss:\nargminS;\u0015X\n(x;y)2Y( (x;y)\u0000\u000e(S(x;y)\u0000\u0015))2;\n\u000e(t) =\u001a1t>0\n0else;\n (p;q) =\u001a\n1 (p;q)2Yeye\n0else;(1)\nwhere the saliency map Sis taken as a binary classiﬁer,\nand\u0015is the classiﬁcation threshold. In such context, the goal\nis to learn a model which can generate the optimal Sthat\ncan best discriminate the ground-truth eye-ﬁxation points and\nthose random ones. For theoretical convenience, most works\navoid the optimization of \u0015by introducing Area Under ROC\nCurve (AUC ) as the ﬁnal performance measure.\nDifferent from traditional binary classiﬁcation problem, the\ndistribution of the positive samples in eye-ﬁxation prediction\ntask is very sparse, while the distribution of the negatives is\nmuch denser. Thus, direct use of the discrete ﬁxation points\nmight not be the most optimal solution.\nAs discussed in [25], the ground truth eye-ﬁxations could\nbe assumed to be the observations sampled from a latent\ndistribution. Thus, one can convert discrete ﬁxation points  \nto a probabilistic density map  0via convolution with a 2D\nGaussian kernel G\u001b. Let 0= \u0003G\u001bbe the ground-truth\nsaliency map, the above optimization problem can be further\nupdated as follows:\nargminSX\nx;y(S(x;y)\u0000 0(x;y))2\n(2)\nHere, Mean Square Error ( MSE ) is introduced as the\nloss function because it has a simple form and can be well\nexplained. However, other complex loss functions can also be\nadopted, e.g. Softmax, Entropy and Information Gain loss.\nB. Overview of the Proposed Framework\nIt has been widely accepted that visual saliency can be\ndriven by both bottom-up (unsupervised) and top-down (su-\npervised) cues. Thus, it is important to take both parts into\nconsideration to construct a plausible and effective model. As\nshown in Figure 2, the proposed saliency framework mainly\n4\nMulti-Scale \nAdaptive \nSparse Coding\nInformation Max\nMaxima NormInput Image\nFeature MapsContrast-Aware SaliencySCAFI Saliency\nIII -Contrast Path\nConv1_1\nConv1_2\nConv2_1\nConv2_2\nConv5_1\nConv5_2\nConv5_3\nWeighted FusionSemantic-Aware Saliency\nFeature MapsII -Semantic Path\n(Feature Integration)\nVGG-16 Pre-trained on ImageNet\nLocal Response NormConvolution 3 to 8\nSCA Saliency\nI-Semantic Path\n(End-To-End Version)\nResized InputConv5 Conv2 Conv1\nFig. 2. Semantic and Contrast-Aware Saliency Model. Part I : An End-to-End deep neural network customized as an alternative version of the semantic-aware\nsaliency ( SAS).Part II: A deep feature integration module as the default SAS pathway, which directly collects and aggregates 2D feature maps from the\nconvolutional layers of pre-trained VGG net [45]. Part III : A contrast-aware saliency ( CAS ) pathway that discovers high contrast salient patterns within the\nimage context based on multi-scale sparse representation and information maximization. The temporal outputs of the SAS andCAS pathways are all resized\nto the original image size and then integrated using maxima normalization. The integration of IandIIIcorresponds to our previous model SCA , while the\ncombination of IIandIIIresults in our new model SCAFI . The experiments show that the new SCAFI model performs much better than the SCA model\nas well as the other state-of-the-arts.\ncontains two independent pathways. i.e. the Semantic-Aware\nSaliency ( SAS) and the Contrast-Aware Saliency ( CAS ). The\nCAS pathway is based on on-line sparse feature learning and\ninformation maximization, which learns an adaptive represen-\ntation for the input image and discovers the high contrast\npatterns within the visual context. The SAS pathway aims\nto capture the attractive semantic information in images,\nespecially for the presence of meaningful objects and object\nparts such as human faces. The two pathways characterize\nboth long-term (semantics in natural images) and short-term\n(contrast in single image) attention cues and are integrated\nusing maxima normalization. We provide two different SAS\nimplementations including an End-to-End deep neural network\nsolution and a dynamic feature integration solution, resulting\nin the SCA andSCAFI model respectively.\nC. Semantic-Aware Saliency based on End-to-End Network\nWe ﬁrst introduce a straight-forward implementation of\nsemantic-aware saliency using a customized End-to-End deep\nconvolutional network. This End-to-End SAS module is\nderived from VGG [45], an existing deep convnet originally\ntrained for large-scale image classiﬁcation. The VGG features\nlearned from large-scale dataset such as ImageNet are highly\ncorrelated with the semantic information such as objects (deep-\nlayer) or object parts (shallow-layer). There are many appli-\ncations that are based on the pre-trained VGG net, covering\nboth low-level tasks like edge prediction [47] and high-level\nproblems such as video event detection [48].Figure 2 ( Part I ) shows the architecture of the End-to-End\nSAS network. We follow a similar parameter setting of [8],\nwhich used the ﬁrst 3 weight layers of a pre-trained VGG\nnet, followed by a pooling layer and 6 convolutional layers.\nA deconvolution layer is adopted at the end of the network\nto obtain the semantic-aware saliency map that matches the\nsize of the input. For any input image, we resize its size to\n[240\u0002320] because human beings can recognize most of the\nimportant objects in images at this resolution. Besides, a larger\nresolution means much more parameters and will signiﬁcantly\nincrease the training and testing time.\nWe used the data from SALICON dataset to train our\nSAS net.SALICON is currently the largest dataset available\nfor saliency prediction task, which provides 10K images for\ntraining and 5K images for testing. Compared to traditional\neye-ﬁxation datasets, SALICON is much larger in size, which\nis more suitable for the training of deep convnet with large\nnumbers of parameters. However, the ﬁxation labels of SAL-\nICON are obtained from mouse-clicks instead of using eye-\ntracking devices, which means they are not pure ground-truth\nand this might produce some unknown side-effects.\nWe train the SAS network based on 9K images from the\nSALICON training set and use the rest 1K for validation.\nThe ﬁrst 3 convolutional layers are initialized based on the\npre-trained VGG net and the remaining layers are initialized\nrandomly. Standard preprocessing procedures are adopted in-\ncluding mean-subtraction and [-1,1] normalization on both the\ninput images and the ground-truth saliency maps. For training,\nwe adopted stochastic gradient descent with Euclidean loss\n5\nusing a batch size of 2 images for 24K iterations. L2weight\nregularizer was used for weight decay and the learning rate\nwas halved after every 100 iterations.\nThe presented End-to-End SAS network aims to capture\nthe semantic information in images, especially for the presence\nof meaningful objects and object parts. The network is very\nlight compared to those designed for object recognition [45]\nbut much more complex than traditional feature-based saliency\nmodels. Some intermediate results generated based on the\nproposed SAS net are presented in Figure 5.\nD. Semantic-Aware Saliency by Feature Integration\nInspired by the Feature Integration theory [32] of visual at-\ntention, we propose an alternative solution for the computation\nof semantic-aware saliency, which directly utilizes the feature\nmaps from the convolutional layers of VGG net to generate\ntheSAS saliency map.\nThe architecture of our dynamic feature integration model\nis illustrated in Figure 2 ( Part II ). After preprocessing, the\nRGB input image is fed into the pre-trained VGG network\n[45]. Then we collect feature maps from all the convolutional\nlayers except the ﬁnal three fully connected layers. Each ﬁlter\nis corresponding to a single feature map generated based on\nthe output of its Relu layer. All feature maps are resized\nto match the spatial resolution of the input. Figure 3 shows\nsome examples of the extracted feature maps. It clearly shows\nthat the VGG features can effectively highlight the semantic\nobjects, e.g. car wheels and faces, in each input image.\nAccording to the layer conﬁguration of the original VGG\nnet, the extracted feature maps can be further classiﬁed into\n5 categories, each of which corresponds to a feature set at a\ncertain spatial scale. Table I shows the key properties of each\nfeature category, including the number of features, the original\nsize of the feature maps and the size of the corresponding\nreceptive ﬁeld. As visualized in Figure 4, feature maps ex-\ntracted form different layers describe the image contents at\ndifferent scales and in different views. The feature maps of the\nshallow layers contain more structural details such as edges\nand textures, while those from the deep layers highlight more\nabout the object and object parts with speciﬁc semantics.\nTABLE I\nTHEPROPERTY OF CNN F EATURES FROM VGG-16\nLayer conv1 conv2 conv3 conv4 conv5\nMap Size 224\u0002224 112\u0002112 56\u000256 28\u000228 14\u000214\nMap Number 128 256 768 1536 1536\nReceptive Field 5 14 40 92 196\nWe adopt a two-step procesure to generate the SAS saliency\nmap based on the above VGG deep features. Firstly, we\naggregate feature maps within each individual scale. Let Clk\nbe the k-th feature map of layer landClk(x;y)denotes the\nfeature response at location (x;y). Examples of the feature maps extracted using pre-trained VGG\nnetwork. It clearly shows that the VGG features can effectively highlight\nimportant objects with certain semantics. e.g. car wheels (#1) and faces (#2).\nConv5                 Conv4                Conv3                 Conv2                 Conv1               Fixations        Input\nFig. 4. Feature maps extracted from different layers of the pre-trained VGG\nnetwork. The shallow layers contain more structural details while the deep\nlayers highlight more about the object and object parts with speciﬁc semantics.\nSl(x;y) =X\nkPlk(x;y);\nPlk(x;y) =eM\u0000Clk(x;y)\nP\nx;yeM\u0000Clk(x;y);(3)\nwhereM=maxx;yClk(x;y)is the maximum of Clk,Plk\nis the normalized map serving as a probability distribution\ninferred by Clk. We may design some speciﬁc metrics to\ndetermine the weight of each individual P. However, we found\nin later experiments that using layer-wise weights is more\n6\nconvenient for feature aggregation since high-level semantics\nare mainly encoded in deeper layers.\nThe second step is to aggregate the saliency information\nacross all the scales. Speciﬁcally, the ﬁnal SAS saliency map\nSSASis obtained by a linear combination of the saliency maps\nfrom all layers:\nSSAS(x;y) =X\nlwlSl(x;y):(4)\nThe combination weight w= [w1;w2;:::w 5]can be initialized\nusing different strategies. An equal weighting strategy is\nsuitable for general usage, where the SAS module is working\nas a single model. A spare setting where one scale is given\na very high weight is more reasonable if the SAS saliency\nis serving as a sub-module of an integrated model. We show\nmore experimental evidence in Sec. IV.\nE. Contrast-Aware Saliency\nWhen too much semantic information is presented in the\nstimuli, e.g. lots of similar cars running by, human visual\nattention is more likely to be attracted by the stimulus with\nhigher feature contrast, e.g. a car with a unique color. In such\nscenarios, the inﬂuence of high-level semantics becomes less\nsigniﬁcant, and it’s more reliable to use data-driven approaches\nfor the estimation of saliency. This intuition leads to the\nsecond component of our saliency model, namely contrast-\naware saliency ( CAS , illustrated in Figure 2 Part III ), which\nis implemented based on multi-scale adaptive sparse represen-\ntation and information maximization.\n1) Multi-Scale Adaptive Sparse Feature Extraction: Given\nan imageI, we ﬁrst turn it into a multi-scale patch-based\nrepresentationfXiji= 1;2;3;4gby scanning Iwith multiple\nsliding windows (window size Bi2f1;3;5;7g) from the top-\nleft to the bottom-right. Xiis stored as a Mi\u0002Nimatrix,\nwhere each column vector corresponds to a reshaped RGB\nimage patch ( Mi=Bi\u0002Bi\u00023is the size of each patch,\nNiis the total number of patches). For each Xi, we apply\nIndependent Component Analysis ( ICA) [49]4to generate a\ncomplete sparse dictionary Wi= [wi\n1;wi\n2;:::;wi\nMi], where\neach basis is an independent component of Xi.\nGiven wi\nj2Wias thej-th basis at scale i, we can generate\na feature vector Fi\njby treating wi\njas a linear ﬁlter:\nFi\nj=wi\njTXi (5)\nwhere Fi\nj(k)denotes the response value of k-th patch for the\nj-th basis at scale i.\n2) Saliency by Information Maximization: Inspired by [18],\nwe measure the contrast-aware saliency of each patch by\nthe self-information of its multi-scale sparse feature vector.\nSpeciﬁcally, the CAS saliency value of the t-th patch at scale\niis deﬁned as:\nSi(t) =\u0000logY\njpi;j(Fi\nj(t))\n=\u0000X\njlogpi;j(Fi\nj(t));(6)\n4FastICA : https://research.ics.aalto.ﬁ/ica/fastica/wherepi;j(:)is the probability density function of the j-\nth feature at scale i.pi;jcan be directly estimated using\nhistogram method. We can obtain multiple saliency maps\nfocusing on different salient patterns at various scales. The\nﬁnal CAS mapSCASis obtained by summing up all the single-\nscale CAS saliency:\nSCAS(x;y) =X\niS0\ni(x;y);(7)\nwhereS0\niis a 2D version of Si. Here we omit the combination\nweights because the multi-scale sparse representation does not\nencode any semantic information and an equally weighted\ncombination can uniformly characterize the contrasts across\ndifferent scales.\nF . Integration of the Two Pathways\nFusing saliency detection results of multiple models has\nbeen recognized as a challenging task since the candidate\nmodels are usually developed based on different cues or\nassumptions. Fortunately, in our case, the integration problem\nis relatively easier since we only consider the outputs from two\npathways. As there is no prior knowledge or other top-down\nguidance can be used, it’s safer to utilize the map statistics\nto determine the importance of each pathway. Intuitively, in\nthe ﬁnal integration stage, we combine the results from two\npathways by summing them up after Maxima Normalization\n(MN) (Algorithm 1):\nSSCA=Nmax(SSAS-N ) +Nmax(SCAS);\nSSCAFI =Nmax(SSAS) +Nmax(SCAS);(8)\nwhereSSAS-N is the output of the End-to-End SAS network\n(Sec. III-C), SSASandSCASare generated using Eqn. 4 and\nEqn. 7 respectively. Figure 5 shows some visual examples\nof the intermediate SAS-N ,SAS and CAS maps. It clearly\nillustrates the preferences and highlights of the two pathways\nin different image context. The SAS map highlights semantics\nsuch as faces and object parts, while the CAS map emphasizes\nthose visual contents with high local contrast. Intuitively, SAS\nseems to be more accurate then SAS-N for the localization of\nsemantic contents.\nAlgorithm 1: Maxima Normalization Nmax(S;t)\nInput: 2D intensity map S, thresh of local maxima t= 0:1\nOutput: Normalized Saliency Map SN\n1:Set the number of local maxima NM= 0\n2:Set the sum of the maxima VM= 0\n3:Set Global Maximum GM= 1\n4:Normalizing the values in Sto a ﬁxed range [0...
