SSAScan localize the semantic objects more accurately.\nThe Maxima Normalization operator Nmax(:)was origi-\nnally proposed for the integration of conspicuous maps from\nmultiple feature channels [5], which has been demonstrated\nvery effective and has a very convincing psychological expla-\nnation. In addition to the MN integration strategy, we also test\ntwo alternative methods in the experiment: Average-Pooling\n(AP) and Max-Pooling ( MP) [50].\nIV. E XPERIMENTS\nWe evaluate our model in two tasks: 1) prediction of human\neye ﬁxation and 2) response to artiﬁcial images with controlled\nsalient patterns. All experiments are based on public available\ndatasets and the saliency maps generated by all the tested\nmodels can be downloaded from our project page5.\nA. Datasets and Evaluation Metric\nFor the eye-ﬁxation prediction task, we used 6 public avail-\nable dataset including: Bruce [18], Cerf [51] , ImgSal [52],\nJudd [46], PASCAL-S [27] and SALICON [44]. Each dataset\ncontains a group of natural images and the corresponding eye-\nﬁxations captured from human subjects. Note that, among the\n6 datasets, SALICON is the largest in size but its eye-ﬁxation\ndata is labeled by mouse clicks instead of using eye-tracking\ndevices. Thus in our experiment, we use SALICON to train\nour End-to-End semantic-aware saliency module and apply the\nrest 5 dataset for performance evaluation. Table II show some\nbasic statistics of the 6 dataset. More details of the datasets\ncan be found in [27] and [44].\nMany evaluation metrics have been developed to measure\nthe quality of saliency maps in predicting human eye-ﬁxations.\nFollowing the procedure of the recent benchmarking surveys\n[1], [27], we use the shufﬂed Area Under ROC curve ( sAUC )\n[53] as the major evaluation metric in our experiment. The\noriginal AUC metric scores a saliency map according to its\nability to separate the ground-truth eye ﬁxations from random\n5https://sites.google.com/view/xssunTABLE II\nSTATISTICS OF THE 6EYE-FIXATION DATASET .\nDataSet Images Subjects Device Year\nBruce [18] 120 20 Eye-Tracker 2006\nCerf [51] 200 7 Eye-Tracker 2008\nImgSal [52] 235 21 Eye-Tracker 2013\nJudd[46] 1003 15 Eye-Tracker 2009\nPASCAL-S [27] 850 8 Eye-Tracker 2014\nSALICON [44] 15000 16 Mouse-Click 2015\nTABLE III\nOVERVIEW OF THE TESTED MODELS .\nName Method Year Fps\nHeuristic Methods\nITTI [5] Integration of Low-level Features 1998 5.1\nAIM [18] Information Maximization 2006 0.31\nGBVS [33] Random Walk on Graph 2006 3.0\nDV A [19] Incremental Coding Length 2008 33\nSUN [54] Sparse Coding and Natural Statistics 2008 3.1\nSIM [55] Low-level Vision Model 2011 0.55\nQDCT [56] Quaternion-based DCT 2012 110\nSIG [17] Image Signature 2012 16\nSGP [21] Super-Gaussian Component Pursuit 2014 4.6\nA WS [31] Dynamic Whitening 2017 0.73\nSCAFI (Ours ) Semantic and Contrast Integration 2018 2.0\nLearning-based Methods\nSAL [57] Open Code of SALICON[24] 2015 0.03\nDeepGazeII [9] Pretrained VGG + Read-out Net 2016 -\nDPN [8] Shallow and Deep Neural Network 2016 0.33\nSCA [26] Integration of Two Saliency Cue 2017 0.32\npoints. In sAUC , positive samples are taken from the eye-\nﬁxation of the test image, whereas the negative ones are\nsampled from other images. We use the GPU implementation\nofsAUC from [27] for the experiments. Post-processing pa-\nrameters (e.g. blur kernel) are also optimized for each method\nto ensure a fair comparison.\nIn addition to the eye-ﬁxation datasets, we also test the\nmodels on a small group of artiﬁcial images consisting of\ncontrolled salient patterns. Most of the images are made based\non solid evidence found in psychological experiments such as\nthe preattentive features. Thus, we can further evaluate the\nplausibility of a model by checking whether it can produce a\nreasonable response to such images.\nB. Results on Eye-Fixation Prediction\nWe have compared our proposed model with several classic\nsaliency prediction algorithms including: ITTI [5],AIM [18],\nGBVS [33], DV A [19], SUN [54], SIM [55], QDCT [56] SIG\n[17], SGP [21] and A WS [31]. Despite the heuristic methods,\nwe’ve also compared to some recent learning-based CNN\nmodels, including SAL [24] (implemented using OpenSalicon\n[57]), DPN [8] and our previous SCA [26] model. Untill now,\nSAL model is still one of the best according to the MIT\nsaliency benchmark [58]. While DPN was the winner of the\nSaliency Detection competition in LSUN Challenge (2015).\nSome details of the baseline models are presented in Table III\nincluding their main ideas and the processing speed. Since the\nDPN andSCA model are partially trained on the SALICON\ndataset, we mainly report and discuss the evaluation results on\nthe other 5 datasets.\n8\nBruce#29             Bruce#114               Cerf#25               Cerf#37           Judd#32          Judd#44             Judd# 615              ImgSal#2            ImgSal#18            Pasca l#242          Pascal#262         SIM       SIG        AIM       AWS             SGP      DPN         SCA      SAL             SCAFI    Fixation  Input\nFig. 6. Visual comparisons of different saliency models. The red dot in each saliency map indicates the location of the maximum saliency. The proposed\nSCAFI model can effectively locate the most salient patterns in various conditions, while other approaches are more likely to be inﬂuenced by complex\nbackgrounds (Judd #615 , Pascal #242 ) and size variations of the salient objects (Bruce #114 , Cerf #37, ImgSal #2).\nFigure 6 shows some representative visual examples of the\nsaliency maps generated by the tested models for various\nnatural scenes. In each map, we use a red dot to highlight\nthe location of the maximum saliency value. Compared to\nthe baselines, the proposed SCAFI model is much more\nreliable and can accurately locate the most salient pattern\nin each image under different interference. Most traditional\napproaches failed when the salient object is small (Cerf #37)\nor with a cluttered background (Judd #615 , Pascal #242 ).\n1) Overall sAUC Performance: Figure 7 and Table IV\npresents the main sAUC results. We show the performance\ncurves of the tested models over different blur kernel \u001b. Table\nIV gives the best sAUC score of each model under its optimal\n\u001b. Figure 8 uses bars to show the overall sAUC performance\nof the tested models on all the datasets. Our proposed SCAFI\nachieved the best sAUC score among the heuristic models\nand showed competitive performance with the best learning-\nbased model ( SAL ). It’s also important to note that the deep\nmodels, including DPN ,SCA ,SAL andSCAFI , signiﬁcantly\noutperform traditional approaches on the two largest datasets,\nJudd andPASCAL . This is quite reasonable because all these\ndeep models apply pre-trained deep neural network to embed\nsemantic cues into the saliency computation process.\n2) Inﬂuence of The Integration Strategies: Table V shows\nthe optimal sAUC score of SCA and SCAFI with Maxima\nMaximization ( MN) and the other two alternative integration\nstrategies, including AP (average pooling), and MP (max\npooling). Overall, MN performs the best, but only slightly\nbetter than AP.MN and APare much better than MP on\nall datasets. It is also worth mentioning that MN works more\nreliably for SCAFI compared to the previous SCA model.3) Inﬂuence of CAS and the SAS weights: In Eqn. 4,\nwe introduce a weighting parameter wwhich balances the\nimportance of features from different VGG layers. We evaluate\n6 intuitive conﬁgurations of w, which corresponds to 5 groups\nof single-scale VGG features and a combination of all VGG\nfeatures. Figure 9 and Table VII shows the sAUC performance\nofSAS (Eqn. 4 as a single model), CAS (Eqn. 6) and\nSCAFI (Eqn. 8) with different SAS weight w(Table VI).\nForSAS,walloutperforms all others on 3 datasets including\nBruce ,ImgSal and Judd , butw5achieves the best overall\nperformance. As a combination of SAS andCAS ,SCAFI with\nw5consistently outperforms the others on all 5 dataset. These\nresults indicate that the Conv5 layer of VGG net contains\nthe best complementary semantic information for our CAS\nmodule, and their combination leads to the best accuracy for\nhuman eye-ﬁxation prediction task.\n4) Saliency Prediction Speed: All the tested models are\nimplemented on a single machine with an i7-7700k 4.20GHz\nCPU, 32G RAM, and a Titan X Pascal GPU. As shown in\nTable III, he speed of the proposed SCAFI is approximately\n2 fps which is acceptable for most of the potential applications,\ne.g. image editing and enhancement. Note that, as a heuristic\nmodel, SCAFI can run 70\u0002faster than SAL with competitive\nsAUC performance.\nC. In comparison, DPN ,DeepGazeII ,\nand SAL only made the most recognizable objects pop out\nwhich merely considered the object-level contrast given the\nimage context. As we discussed in Sec. I, objects indeed attract\nhuman attention and draw a large proportion of eye-ﬁxations,\nyet an object detector alone doesn’t make a robust eye-ﬁxationpredictor because the context of the visual environment is\nsometimes more important for human vision system to decide\nthe next move of the eyes.\nV. C ONCLUSION\nIn this paper, we proposed a heuristic framework to inte-\ngrate both semantic-aware and contrast-aware saliency which\ncombines bottom-up and top-down cues simultaneously for\neffective eye ﬁxation prediction. As the middle results, the\nSAS andCAS maps generated by the two pathways clearly\nshow their preference and highlights in the input image. Exper-\nimental results on 5 benchmark datasets and artiﬁcial images\ndemonstrate the superior performance and better plausibility\nof the proposed SCAFI model over both classic approaches\nand the recent deep models.\n11\nclosure     intersection   intensity       curve   orientation     c olor \nInput       AIM         DPN     DeepGazeII SAL                   SC A           SCAFI‐w1SCAFI‐w2       SCAFI‐w3            SCAFI‐w4         SCAFI‐w5SCAFI‐wall\n(a) Response to artiﬁcal salient patterns\n#8            #7                #6             #5                #4               #3 #2      #1\nInput       AIM         DPN     DeepGazeII SAL                   SC A           SCAFI‐w1SCAFI‐w2       SCAFI‐w3            SCAFI‐w4         SCAFI‐w5SCAFI‐wall\n(b) Response to natural images with controlled salient patterns\nFig. 11. {"id": "2305.16445v1", "paper_content": "SoundSieve: Seconds-Long Audio Event Recognition on\nIntermittently-Powered Systems\nMahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon\nDepartment of Computer Science\nUniversity of North Carolina at Chapel Hill\nChapel Hill, NC, USA\n{mahathir,yubo,zywang,nirjon}@cs.unc.edu\nABSTRACT\nA fundamental problem of every intermittently-powered sensing\nsystem is that signals acquired by these systems over a longer pe-\nriod in time are also intermittent . As a consequence, these systems\nfail to capture parts of a longer-duration event that spans over\nmultiple charge-discharge cycles of the capacitor that stores the\nharvested energy. From an application’s perspective, this is viewed\nas sporadic bursts of missing values in the input data – which may\nnot be recoverable using statistical interpolation or imputation\nmethods. In this paper, we study this problem in the light of an\nintermittent audio classification system and design an end-to-end\nsystem – SoundSieve – that is capable of accurately classifying audio\nevents that span multiple on-off cycles of the intermittent system.\nSoundSieve employs an offline audio analyzer that learns to iden-\ntify and predict important segments of an audio clip that must be\nsampled to ensure accurate classification of the audio. At runtime,\nSoundSieve employs a lightweight, energy- and content-aware au-\ndio sampler that decides when the system should wake up to capture\nthe next chunk of audio; and a lightweight, intermittence-aware\naudio classifier that performs imputation and on-device inference.\nThrough extensive evaluations using popular audio datasets as well\nas real systems, we demonstrate that SoundSieve yields 5%–30%\nmore accurate inference results than the state-of-the-art.\nCCS CONCEPTS\n•Computer systems organization →Embedded software .\nKEYWORDS\nAudio perforation, scheduling, sampling, classification\nACM Reference Format:\nMahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon. 2023. Sound-\nSieve: Seconds-Long Audio Event Recognition on Intermittently-Powered\nSystems. In The 21st Annual International Conference on Mobile Systems,\nApplications and Services (MobiSys ’23), June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland.\nACM, New York, NY, USA, 14 pages. https://doi.org/10.1145/3581791.3596859\nPermission to make digital or hard copies of all or part of this work for personal or\nclassroom use is granted without fee provided that copies are not made or distributed\nfor profit or commercial advantage and that copies bear this notice and the full citation\non the first page. Copyrights for components of this work owned by others than the\nauthor(s) must be honored. Abstracting with credit is permitted. To copy otherwise, or\nrepublish, to post on servers or to redistribute to lists, requires prior specific permission\nand/or a fee. Request permissions from permissions@acm.org.\nMobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland\n©2023 Copyright held by the owner/author(s). Publication rights licensed to ACM.\nACM ISBN 979-8-4007-0110-8/23/06. . . $15.00\nhttps://doi.org/10.1145/3581791.35968591 INTRODUCTION\nAs batteryless computing systems continue to mature, we see a\ngradual shift in research from developing tools and platforms for\nenergy harvesting systems [ 12,21,22,60,67], to devising new pro-\ngramming paradigms and runtime systems [ 13,14,23,29], to more\nrecently, implementing machine learning techniques and applica-\ntions [ 26,28,35,47,70] tailored to intermittently-powered systems\nthat run on harvested energy from solar, kinetic, thermal, or RF\nsources. A wide variety of on-device inference-capable intermittent\nsystems have been proposed in the recent literature [ 11,17,20,27,\n34,35,45,46,52,53,56,68,73] that perform on-device inference\nof audio, image, environmental parameters, building activity, and\nhuman activity recognition tasks.\nAudio \nClip\nTypical\nIntermittent\nSystems\nSound\nSievesample\nharvest harvestclassify\nsample\nharvest sleep harvestclassifyImportant\nharvest\nsamplesample\nFigure 1: A typical intermittent system, for lack of content awareness,\nmisses important portions of the audio. SoundSieve samples and\nanalyzes an earlier segment, decides (in real-time) to conserve energy\nby sleeping (and also by harvesting while in sleep mode) and use\nthat energy later to capture the important segment. Note that there\ncan be multiple non-consecutive important segments in an audio.\nWe only show one for illustration.\nUnfortunately, existing intermittent systems have only been\ndemonstrated successful in classifying short-lived events that last\nfor about a few hundred milliseconds [ 52]. This limit is fundamental\nto the design of intermittent systems that require a fixed-sized\ncapacitor to accumulate enough harvested energy for the system to\nwake up and remain active. The system in its active mode consumes\nthe harvested energy to perform application-specific tasks, but soon\nruns out of energy and goes back to sleep. By choosing a larger\n(or smaller) capacitor, the lengths of the active and sleep phases\ncan be increased (or decreased), but generally, there will always\nbe one or more sleep phases when the system is unable to acquire\nany sensor data. This is why state-of-the-art intermittent systems\nimplicitly assume that the event of interest lasts no more than one\ndischarging cycle of the capacitor so that it can be captured fully\nin one active phase of the system. Events that span across multiple\ncharge-discharge cycles of the capacitor are not fully captured byarXiv:2305.16445v1  [cs.SD]  25 May 2023\nMobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland Mahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon\nexisting intermittent systems. Hence, specialized techniques are\nrequired to ensure accurate classification of longer-duration events\non intermittently-powered systems.\nIn this paper, we take up the challenge of audio event classifi-\ncation on intermittent systems where events may last longer than\none charging cycle (and possibly more). To achieve this, we exploit\ntwo empirically-learned properties of audio signals: first, relevance\n– i.e., not every segment of an audio clip contributes equally to\nits classification; and second, imputability – i.e., many segments\nwithin an audio clip can be imputed or estimated to a sufficient\ndetail from other nearby segments.\nThese observations lead us to the design of SoundSieve — a\nsoftware-only, content- and energy-aware solution to seconds-long\naudio event classification on intermittent systems. The key idea\nbehind SoundSieve is its ability to predict and control when an\nintermittent system should wake up from sleep – in order to cap-\nture and process only the relevant, non-imputable segments of the\ninput audio. Unlike existing intermittent systems that continuously\nsample and process audio until the capacitor is fully drained of har-\nvested energy, SoundSieve saves energy by switching to sleep mode\nwhen the incoming audio does not carry information that is new\nor relevant to classification. This strategy increases SoundSieve’s\ndischarge time since the capacitor now can hold charge and use\nthat energy later. Because of this, SoundSieve is able to sense and\nprocess input signals during intervals when existing intermittent\nsystems cannot. This contrast is illustrated in Figure 1.\nAlthough the idea of SoundSieve is simple and intuitive, develop-\ning such a solution is challenging due to severe CPU, memory, and\nenergy constraints of an intermittent system. SoundSieve cannot\nafford computationally expensive sampling techniques that are suit-\nable for battery-powered, advanced mobile systems such as robotic\nexplorers [ 18,32,75]. SoundSieve must sample a small segment of\nthe input audio, analyze its content, and decide whether to con-\ntinue sampling or to go to sleep (and if so, for how long). All of\nthese must happen in real-time, and ideally, without any additional\nenergy overhead. SoundSieve achieves this through a combination\nof offline and online algorithms.\nSoundSieve employs an offline audio analyzer that learns to iden-\ntify and predict important segments of an audio clip that must be\nsampled to ensure accurate classification of the audio clip. At run-\ntime, SoundSieve employs a lightweight, energy- and content-aware\naudio sampler that decides when the system should wake up to\ncapture the next audio segment. The sampler’s decision algorithm\nruns in parallel with the signal acquisition process to ensure that\nthere is no gap in signal acquisition. The decision algorithm pur-\nposefully applies a convolution filter on the sampled audio to make\naccurate sampling decisions based on high-level acoustic features.\nSince this filter is also the first layer of the classifier (by design),\nthere is practically no extra energy overhead of the audio sampler.\nOnce an audio event ends, a lightweight, intermittence-aware audio\nclassifier performs imputation and classification.\nSeveral salient features make SoundSieve unique of its kind. First,\nSoundSieve is the first intermittent system that makes content-\naware sampling and processing decisions to enable long dura-\ntion audio event recognition. Second, SoundSieve is a single-node,\nsoftware-only solution that neither requires multiple coordinatedharvesters, nor any modification to the harvester, nor any addi-\ntional hardware components beyond a basic intermittent computing\nsystem. Third, SoundSieve is agnostic to the harvester and inter-\nmittence pattern. Even battery-powered systems can adopt Sound-\nSieve’s intelligent sensing mechanism to extend their battery-life.\nFourth, SoundSieve’s signal processing framework is generalizable\nbeyond audio. It can inspire future intermittent systems that detect\ncomplex patterns in time-series signals [77, 78] such as video [41]\nand motion sensors [40, 42, 79].\nIn order to evaluate SoundSieve, we conduct dataset-driven ex-\nperiments as well as real-world deployments with these systems.\nIn the dataset-driven experiments, we compare the performance\nof SoundSieve against three baseline solutions, including state-of-\nthe-art intermittent system [ 52], over four datasets that contain\nover 140,000 audio clips from over 125 categories of sounds hav-\ning the duration of up to 4 seconds. We observe that SoundSieve\noutperforms all baselines by a significant margin — SoundSieve\nsuccessfully captures 5%–30% more (relevant) audio segments, and\nas a result, achieves 5%–25% higher inference accuracy than the\nbaseline solutions. For the real-world deployments, we develop a 16-\nbit TI MSP430FR5994-based intermittent system that senses audio\nin real-time and performs on-device inference while being powered\nby solar or RF energy. We implement two applications that involve\nrecognizing voice commands and household activities, respectively.\nWe demonstrate that SoundSieve captures and accurately classifies\n20% more events than the baseline intermittent systems which do\nnot perform content-aware sampling.\n2 EMPIRICAL STUDY\nIn order to understand the effect of missing values caused by inter-\nmittence, we conduct an empirical study on three popular audio\ndatasets: Urban8K [ 64], ESC-50 [ 61], and GSC [ 71]. These datasets\ncontain over 115,000 audio clips of over 95 different categories.\n2.1 Dealing With Missing Values\nA classifier that does not explicitly handle missing values performs\nextremely poorly when it encounters such inputs. This is evident\nin Figure 2 – confirming similar prior studies [ 58] – where we see\n20%–50% drop in inference accuracy (referred to as do nothing )\nwhen 75%of the data are missing.\nUrban8k ESC-50 GSC Average020406080100Accuracy (%)Original\nDo NothingData Imputation\nData Augmentation\nFigure 2: Imputation and augmentation increase accuracy, but cannot\nmaximize the gain in accuracy by themselves. The percentage of\nmissing values in the experiment is 75%.\nTo address this issue, there are two generic classes of solutions in\nthe literature: (a) estimating (imputing) the missing values (before\nclassification) using statistical or neural methods, and (b) training\nthe classifier on an augmented dataset where missing values are\nsynthetically introduced in the training examples. Although imple-\nmenting any or both of these techniques improve the classification\nSoundSieve: Seconds-Long Audio Event Recognition on Intermittently-Powered Systems MobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland\naccuracy – referred to as imputation andaugmentation , respectively\n– there is still a large gap of 5%–40% in accuracy that cannot be\nregained. Furthermore, the classifier used in this experiment is\na state-of-the-art CNN having 1008 filters, which is not feasible\nfor intermittent systems. In conclusion, data imputation and/or\ndata augmentation cannot completely solve the intermittent\naudio classification problem. We need to complement them\nwith audio content-aware intelligent sampling at the front\nto maximize their performance.\n2.2 Feasibility of Content-Aware Sampling\nAlthough an intermittent system does not have complete control\nover the harvesting (charging) cycles, which depend on the en-\nergy source, once charged, the system can decide when to use that\nharvested energy. This creates an opportunity for the system to\nselectively sample segments of the audio that are more informative\nand relevant to audio classification.\nWe conduct another study to understand what percentage of an\naudio clip must be sampled so that we have enough information to\nclassify a clip correctly. To conduct this study, we (logically) divide\neach clip into non-overlapping 100ms segments and search for the\nsmallest set of Kmost informative segments that must be sampled\nto correctly classify the clip. We keep the Kselected segments intact,\nand impute the rest of the segments prior to classifying the clip.\nThe outcome of this experiment is summarized in Table 1.\nSound Category #Clips K=10% K=30% K=50% K=70% K=90%\nVoice Command 600 64.20% 81.50% 88.70% 100.00% 100.00%\nVibration 120 56.62% 81.18% 92.31% 93.47% 94.20%\nAlarm 85 54.62% 85.09% 91.91% 93.11% 93.50%\nChildren 80 56.62% 88.82% 92.19% 94.61% 95.95%\nMusic 76 48.52% 82.29% 89.61% 91.47% 94.14%\nPet 76 52.27% 83.61% 92.10% 94.27% 95.10%\nAnimal 65 54.68% 90.63% 92.21% 93.00% 100.00%\nBursts 16 51.13% 65.90% 81.81% 85.20% 92.00%\nLaundry 16 54.10% 69.70% 83.10% 89.40% 98.00%\nSleep 16 56.21% 72.27% 80.53% 88.96% 100.00%\nWellness 16 64.32% 85.42% 88.78% 100.00% 100.00%\nBathroom 12 61.11% 83.33% 90.00% 100.00% 100.00%\nDrink 12 55.32% 67.40% 84.18% 86.24% 95.00%\nOverall 1190 51.47% 85.91% 89.60% 92.30% 96.60%\nTable 1: The percentage of clips that are classified correctly\nwhen we sample K%most informative segments.\nEach row of Table 1 is a cumulative distribution of Kfor a certain\naudio category, where Kdenotes the minimum number of most\ninformative segments to sample to classify an audio correctly. For an intermittent system, from its\ncharge and discharge times, we can estimate what fraction of the\ntime the system can sample, and then estimate their expected infer-\nence accuracy from these distributions. These datasets, however,\nare trimmed and preprocessed to remove unwanted sounds. In conclusion, not all audio segments being\nequally informative, a content-aware sampler on a resource-\nconstrained sensing system can select a subset of informative\naudio segments to maximize their ability to correctly classify\nlong-duration audio clips.\n2.3 Sparsity of Audio Events\nUsing a large capacitor for intermittent audio event classification\nensures continuous energy supply, but can result in missed events\nif charging time is longer than gaps between consecutive events.\n2 4 6 8 10\nCapacitor size (mF)0204060Missed events (%)C=1 C=1.5 C=2 C=3\nFigure 4: Percentage of audio events that are missed for different\ncapacitor sizes at different energy harvesting rates. In\ncontrast, smaller capacitors, e.g., 100𝜇𝐹, results in as low as 4%\nmissed audio events, but the captured audio events have many\nmissing samples or holes in them – which calls for an intelligent\naudio sampling and processing technique that deals with missing\nsamples in intermittent audio sensing systems.\nMobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland Mahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon\nSegment\nImportance\nAudio \nClip\nSTFT\nSegments\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\n𝑋1𝑋2𝑋3 𝑋𝑛\n𝜓1𝜓2𝜓3𝜓𝑛…\n…\n𝑓𝑋𝑡→𝜓𝑡+1,𝜓𝑡+2,…,𝜓𝑡+𝑇Importance\nPredictionAudio Analysis and Modeling\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\n𝑥1𝑥2𝑥3 𝑥𝑛\n(a) Offline Phase\nAudio Sensing and Inference\nAudio\nSensor\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nSampling\nController\nSpectral\nFeatureremaining\nenergy\nImputation\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nClass Label\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\n𝑋1𝑋2𝑋𝑘… (b) Online Phase\nFigure 5: SoundSieve consists of two phases: (a) an offline phase for audio analysis and modeling, and (b) an online (real-time) phase for audio\nsensing and inference on an intermittent system.\n3 OVERVIEW OF SOUNDSIEVE\nSoundSieve is an audio sensing and inference framework that\nenables seconds-long audio event recognition on intermittently-\npowered systems. At the heart of SoundSieve is a lightweight,\ncontent-aware sampling algorithm that decides (in real-time) when\nthe system should wake up from sleep to actively sample the next\nfew segments of the incoming audio. SoundSieve’s sampling ob-\njective is to maximize the classification accuracy by capturing the\nmost informative audio segments, given the remaining charge in\nthe capacitor, which limits the maximum number of segments the\nsystem can sample before it runs out of harvested energy.\nSoundSieve is a software-only solution which does not require\nspecialized hardware or multiple collaborating nodes. The frame-\nwork consists of two phases: (a) an offline phase for audio analysis\nand modeling, and (b) an online (real-time) phase for audio sensing\nand inference on an intermittent system. The offline phase involves\nprocessing a large dataset of audio recordings to extract features\nand train machine learning models for both audio event sampling\nand classification. The online phase involves deploying the trained\nmodels to an intermittent system, where the system can detect\naudio events in real-time. The online phase operates on a continu-\nous cycle of energy harvesting, sensing, and classification. Figure 5\nshows both phases along with the major processing steps in them.\n3.1 Audio Analysis and Modeling\nAudio Segment Importance. SoundSieve logically divides au-\ndio clips into non-overlapping 100ms segments , and processes one\nsegment at a time. The segment size is empirically determined\nto optimize the system’s ability to extract acoustic features un-\nder different energy harvesting conditions. Each audio segment\nis assigned an importance score that denotes how important that\nsegment is for accurate classification of the clip. During the offline\nphase of SoundSieve, the importance score of each 100ms segment\nof every audio clip in the dataset is computed.Predicting Segment Importance Scores. In order for SoundSieve\nto decide which segments to capture next, the system must be able\nto predict the importance of the next few segments in the incoming\naudio. To enable this, during the offline phase of SoundSieve, a\nsimple regression network (which runs on the intermittent platform\nin real-time) is trained. The predictor predicts the importance score\nof up to 5next segments, given the frequency domain features of\nthe audio segment that has just been read.\n3.2 Audio Sensing and Inference\nSampling. SoundSieve’s audio processing system is triggered by\nthe microphone, which remains in ultra-low-power listening mode\nand sends an interrupt to the microcontroller whenever a sound is\ndetected. SoundSieve reads an audio segment and extracts its spec-\ntral features. The sampling controller uses these features and the\noffline-trained importance predictor to identify potential candidate\nsegments to read, and based on the remaining charge in the energy\nbuffer, it decides whether (and for how long) it should go into to\nthe low-power mode before it samples the next segment.\nImputation. Once the audio event ends, prior to classifying, the\nmissing segments in the sampled audio are imputed to estimate the\nmissing values. A lightweight, hierarchical interpolation technique\nis used to impute the missing segments. Note that even though\nthe missing segments are less important (according to the segment\nimportance score predictor), SoundSieve still imputes them to form\na continuous audio clip which can be fed to a neural network.\nClassification. An offline-trained convolutional neural network\n(CNN) is used to classify the audio. The classifier is trained on an\naugmented dataset where missing values are synthetically intro-\nduced at random locations in the audio clip and then the audio is\nimputed. To be able to classify audio signals of arbitrary lengths, a\nglobal max pooling layer is used after the final convolution layer\nwhich sums out the spatial information, making it robust to the\narbitrary spatial dimension of the input.\nSoundSieve: Seconds-Long Audio Event Recognition on Intermittently-Powered Systems MobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland\n4 AUDIO ANALYSIS AND MODELING\nDuring the offline phase, SoundSieve analyzes each audio clip in the\ndataset to estimate the importance of each segment and to generate\na predictor that is able to predict the importance scores.\n4.1 Audio Segment Importance\nSince harvested energy is scarce, SoundSieve aims to sample only\nthe highly informative segments to make the best use of the energy.\nHence, there has to be an objective measure that quantifies the\nimportance of each segment of an audio clip – which the sampling\ncontroller can use in its segment selection process.\nApproach. A naive way to identify important segments of an\naudio clip is to remove that segment, estimate its value using the\nremaining segments, and then test if this modified audio clip can\nstill be classified correctly by a classifier that correctly classifies the\nunmodified original clip. This technique, however, does not provide\na score that quantifies the importance of the segment.\nSoundSieve determines the importance of each segment of an\naudio clip by analyzing its relative contribution towards the classi-\nfication of the clip. The idea is similar to feature selection problem\nwhen each segment is treated as a feature. A linear regressor takes\nthese features as the input and tries to predict the output – which\nis a value as predicted by a baseline classifier (e.g., a neural net-\nwork). The weights of the linear regressor indicates the relative\nimportance of each feature (i.e. segment) when it tries to mimic the\nbehavior of the baseline classifier.\nAlgorithmic Details. Given, a baseline classifier, 𝑓, that is trained\non the entire dataset; and an audio clip, x, consisting of 𝑛segments,\nfollowing algorithmic steps are followed to quantify the importance\nof each segment:\nStep 1 – Generate a large number of audio clips having missing\nvalues, z𝑖, by randomly turning on and off arbitrary number of\nsegments in x.\nStep 2 – Impute all missing values in z𝑖using a fast, lightweight\nimputation algorithm described later in this paper (Section 5.3).\nStep 3 – Train a linear ridge regressor, 𝑔that minimizes a mea-\nsure of how unfaithful 𝑔is in approximating 𝑓in the locality defined\nby a cosine distance metric. The loss function is as follows:\nargmin\nw∑︁\n𝑖𝑑(x,z𝑖)\u0002\n𝑓(z𝑖)−𝑔(z𝑖;w)\u00032\nHere,𝑑(x,z𝑖)is a distance measure between the original clip, x\nand the augmented clip, z𝑖;wis the learned weight vector whose\nelements{𝑤𝑘}are the desired importance scores of the segments\ninx.\nThese three steps described above are repeated for each audio\nclip in the dataset.\n4.2 Predicting Segment Importance Scores\nSince the sampling controller in SoundSieve has to predict the\nimportance of the next few segments that are yet to be sampled, it\nrequires a predictor that can do so from the knowledge of recently\nsampled audio. As this predictor has to run on the intermittent\nsystem in real-time, it must be lightweight and low-overhead.\n𝜓1𝜓2𝜓3𝜓𝑛\n…Audio \nSegment s\nImportance\nScore s𝑥1𝑥2𝑥3 𝑥𝑛\n𝑥𝑡\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nMissing Value\nPredictionClassification\nFFT CONV FC𝜓𝑡+1,…,𝜓𝑡+𝑇Figure 6: Segment Importance Predictor.\nApproach. Given an observed audio segment at any instant, Sound-\nSieve employs a lightweight neural network to predict the impor-\ntance scores of the next few segments of the audio. The network is\ntrained during the offline phase and used in the online (real-time)\nphase of SoundSieve.\nThe network consists of fully connected layers – which makes\nit fast and low-overhead. The network, however, takes spectral\nfeatures of the audio segment as the input since they carry unique\nsignatures of various types of audio events. Even though the pre-\ndictor uses spectral features, these features are identical to the first\nlayer of the audio classier (described in Section 5.4) which are reused.\nThis is why they do not add extra overhead to the end-to-end audio\nprocessing cost in SoundSieve.\nAlgorithmic Details. The development and training process of\nthe segment score predictor is as follows:\nStep 1 – For each audio clip in the dataset, we randomly take a\nsegment from the clip and take the importance scores of the next\n5 segments. Depending on the length of the audio, the number of\nsegments in a clip can be anything. SoundSieve’s goal is to predict\nthe next 5segments’ importance scores, given the current segment.\nTo train this predictor, from each 𝑛-segment clip,(𝑛−6+1)training\nexamples are generated.\nStep 2 – Segments chosen in the previous step undergo a feature\nextraction process where a 1x4 convolution operation is applied\nafter taking the short-time Fourier transform of the segment.\nStep 3 – The regression neural network is trained using the\nspectral features as the input and the importance scores as the\noutput.\nRationale behind Convolution. The convolution layer has a fil-\nter of dimension (1x4); hence, the filter only convolves along the\nfrequency axis but not the time axis. The reason behind this design\nis three-fold. First, a filter that convolves along the time domain is\nnot feasible since a segment is observed only for a very short period\nin time. Second, due to memory constraints, the dimensions of the\nsampled data needs to be lowered, which is achieved by convolu-\ntion. Third, by using a convolution layer which overlaps with the\naudio classifier – we are able to extract fine-grained information\nfor predicting the importance of next few segments.\n4.3 Global Segment Importance\nAfter computing the local importance of each segment for each\naudio clip, we aggregate the scores to compute a global importance\nscore of the audio segments over the entire dataset. The process\nof computing global importance score of the audio segments is as\nfollows:\nMobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland Mahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon\nStep 1 – The importance score of each segment of each clip are\nnormalized to the range [-1, +1].\nStep 2 – The average of all importance scores for each segment\nover all audio clips in the whole dataset is computed. This gives\nus a global importance score of each audio segment for the entire\ndataset.\nBoth global and local importance scores are used by SoundSieve\nto decide which segment to sample next. The rationale behind hav-\ning a global importance score for each segment is that when none\nof the next few segments have significantly high local importance\nscores or there is a lot of uncertainty in the prediction of the local\nimportance scores, but the system has energy to sample one or more\nsegments, it needs another means to be able to rank the segments.\nThis technique complements the local score-based sampling and\nimproves SoundSieve’s ability to sample informative segments.\n5 AUDIO SENSING AND INFERENCE\nDuring the online (real-time) phase, SoundSieve intelligently sam-\nples audio segments, imputes the missing values, and performs\non-device inference.\n5.1 Sensing and Preprocessing\nSoundSieve remains in its ultra-low-power sleep mode when there\nis no sound in the environment and it has no pending tasks. This is\nenabled by the ultra-low-power listening mode of the microphone\nwhich continuously listens to the environment while being powered\nby harvested energy and sends an interrupt signal to the microcon-\ntroller only when audio activities are detected in the environment.\nThis is when the microcontroller wakes up and switches to active\nmode (subject to availability of adequate harvested energy) to start\nits real-time audio sensing and inference processes.\nSoundSieve samples audio data at the unit of 100ms segments.\nAfter reading an audio segment, it analyzes the segment (as part of\nthe sampling controller algorithm that is described next) to decide\nwhether to read the next segment or to go to sleep. This analy-\nsis process involves computing the spectral features of the audio\nsegment that takes significant amount of time. Hence, SoundSieve\nsamples and pre-processes a segment in parallel, so that sampling\ndecisions can be made in real-time.\nThis is implemented by maintaining a four-element FIFO buffer\nqueue, where each buffer holds 25ms of audio. The key idea is to\npre-process data from a subset of the buffers in the low-energy\naccelerator (LEA) co-processor while the other buffer fills in with\nnew data. The steps are as follows:\nStep 1 – Initially, the first two buffers fill up (25ms + 25ms =\n50ms).\nStep 2 – While the spectral features are extracted on buffers 1\nand 2, in parallel, the third buffer fills up.\nStep 3 – While the spectral features are extracted on buffers 2\nand 3, in parallel, the fourth buffer fills up.\nStep 4 – The spectral features are extracted on buffers 3 and 4.\nBy using these buffers, we compute FFT of 50ms audio, which\nmeans the window length for the STFT is 50ms. We are also moving\nour window by one buffer during each iteration, meaning we are\nusing a hop length of 25 ms. Additionally we are also using a\nconvolution layer of shape (1x4), which means the convolution isdone only in the frequency axis and not in the time axis. Hence it\nis not dependent on the previous or next temporal information.\n5.2 Sampling Strategy\nSoundSieve makes sampling decisions at runtime based on both the\nglobal as well as the local importance scores of the segments. The\nglobal importance scores are used to have an initial plan for segment\nselection and sampling. This initial sampling plan is modified as\nthe system acquires new segments and gets more insights into the\nimportance of the segments based on their local importance scores.\nParameter C. SoundSieve keeps track of its energy harvesting rate\nby computing the ratio, 𝐶between the charging and discharging\ntimes of the capacitor. This ratio characterizes the dynamics of an\nintermittent system. For example, a continuously-powered system\nhas𝐶=0, while any intermittent system has 𝐶>0. If𝐶=1,\nan intermittent system has to charge for the duration of exactly\none segment in order to fully recover the energy it lost by sensing\none segment. Therefore, the value of 𝐶=1.0,1.5,2.0,3.0refers to\n50%,60%,67%,75%missing values in the data, respectively. Gener-\nally,𝐶=1represents outdoor light (1200 lux), 𝐶=2represents\nindoor light (800 lux), and 𝐶=3represents dimly lit room (500 lux)\nand weak RF sources. Recent works on intermittent sensing have\nused a value of 𝐶within similar ranges. For example, [ 52] used\n1<𝐶<3, and [ 7] used 0.8<𝐶<1(outdoor solar), 1.1<𝐶<3\n(indoor solar), and 1.0<𝐶<3.2(indoor RF).\nInitial Sampling Strategy. SoundSieve uses the parameter 𝐶to\nformulate an initial sampling strategy as follows:\nStep 1 – At first the maximum number of segments, 𝑡𝑚𝑎𝑥 that can\nbe sampled is estimated from the current charging-to-discharging\nratio,𝐶. For instance, if 𝐶=2, the system has to wait twice the\namount amount of time in low-power sleep mode to recharge the\ncapacitor to gain back the same amount of energy that is used for\nsampling and processing audio. This sets a limit on the number\nof segments that the system can sample and use in classifying the\naudio event.\nStep 2 – A binary mask is created that tells the system which\nsegment to sample and which to skip. Using both the charge-to-\ndischarge ratio, 𝐶and the global segment importance scores, the\nmask values for the most important 𝑡𝑚𝑎𝑥 segments are set to 1.\nThese are the segments that the system should sense in order to\nmaximize its classification performance.\nStep 3 – In order to account for energy constraints, the mask\nvalues (i.e., the current sampling strategy) are checked to ensure\nthat in no point in time the stored energy is completely exhausted,\nand if so, the mask value for that position is set to 0. This is a\nsituation when the system must stop sampling for lack of energy\neven though the segment is important.\nAdapting the Initial Sampling Strategy. SoundSieve relies on\nthe low-power listening mode of the microphone to remain in\nlow-power mode waiting for an audio event to occur. Once the\nmicrophone interrupts SoundSieve, the system transitions from\nlow-power mode to active mode, and starts sampling and processing\naudio segments of length, 𝑡𝑠𝑒𝑔𝑚𝑒𝑛𝑡 .\nThe system starts with the initial sampling strategy, but the plan\nchanges as it encounters new audio segments. The process is as\nfollows:\nSoundSieve: Seconds-Long Audio Event Recognition on Intermittently-Powered Systems MobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland\nStep 1 – STFT of the audio segment is computed and a 1x4 con-\nvolution is performed on the frequency axis to extract the frequency\ndomain features.\nStep 2 – The segment importance predictor is used to predict\nthe importance of the next 𝑛segments. There are two cases: first, if\nthe importance scores of any segment is higher than an empirically\nobtained threshold, or if the system is sitting idle with full charge,\nit decides to sample that segment that is locally important but may\nnot have been included in the initial sampling plan. Second, if none\nof the importance scores is higher than the threshold, the system\nfollows the initial sampling plan as the default.\nAfter deciding which segment to sample next, unless it is the\nvery next segment, SoundSieve switches to low-power sleep mode\nand wakes itself up when that segment arrives. This process is\nrepeated until the end of audio event.\n5.3 Imputation\nAlthough SoundSieve’s intelligent sampling algorithm has control\nover which segments to sample, it cannot avoid missing segments\nin the captured audio. Prior to classifying an audio clip that has\nmissing values, SoundSieve estimates the missing values using a\nfrequency domain imputation technique.\nApproach. Prior to imputation, SoundSieve transforms time do-\nmain audio signals into frequency domain signals using the fast\nFourier transform (FFT). The rationale behind imputing signals in\nthe frequency domain is that audio events are characterized by their\nfrequency components and the unique characteristics of different\ntypes of audio events are easily observable in the frequency domain.\nFurthermore, the frequency components of an audio event typically\ndo not change drastically within a short period. Hence, interpolat-\ning the signal in the frequency domain is much more effective than\ndoing so in the time domain.\nAlgorithmic Details. Given a sequence of audio segments where\nsome of the segments are null (missing valued), following steps are\nperformed to estimate the missing values:\nStep 1 – Suppose, there are missing segments from time step,\n𝑡𝑠to time step, 𝑡𝑒. Hence, the length of the missing segments is\n𝑡𝑠−𝑡𝑒+1. The imputation process starts from these two ends, 𝑡𝑠\nand𝑡𝑒.\nStep 2 – At each time step, 𝑡, the frequency domain components\nare interpolated using following equations:\n𝑋(𝑡,𝑓)=\u0002\n1−𝑟(𝑡)\u0003\n𝑋(𝑡𝑠−1,𝑓)+𝑟(𝑡)𝑋(𝑡𝑒+1,𝑓) (1)\n𝑟(𝑡)=𝑡−(𝑡𝑠−1)\n(𝑡𝑒+1)−(𝑡𝑠−1)(2)\nStep 3 – The previous step is repeated after the value of 𝑡𝑠has\nbeen increased and the value of 𝑡𝑒has been decreased, until 𝑡𝑠<𝑡𝑒.\n5.4 Inference\nSoundSieve employs a convolutional neural network (CNN) to clas-\nsify the audio event. The CNN consists of a combination of con-\nvolution, max pooling, and fully connected layers. The network\narchitecture is shown in Table 2.\nIteration 1\n Iteration 2𝑡𝑠𝑡𝑒 𝑡𝑠𝑡𝑒Figure 7: Illustration of imputation: during the first iteration, audio\nsegments at time 𝑡𝑠and𝑡𝑒are interpolated, leaving only one segment\nleft for imputation. The process is repeated until all segments are\nimputed.\n#Layer Dimensions Parameters\n1Conv2D 60x32x1 5\n2Conv2D 29x30x2 20\n3Conv2D 12x13x8 152\n4Conv2D 4x4x32 2336\n5Dense 256 8448\n6Dense 10 2056\nTable 2: Neural network architecture (13,017 parameters).\nApproach. The inference process starts when sampling the audio\nevent has completed as determined by a period of silence by the\nmicrophone in its ultra-low-power listening mode.\nAfter sampling has completed and the missing audio segments\nhave been imputed, the resultant short-time Fourier transform\n(STFT) values are converted to Mel-frequency spectrogram to re-\nduce the dimension of the input signal. The spectrogram passes\nthrough to a sequence of convolution and max pooling layers. Af-\nter the last convolution layer, a global max pooling layer is used —\nwhich takes the maximum value across time and frequency domains\nfor each channel, and outputs one value for each of the channels.\nThis design makes it possible for the model to handle audio signals\nof arbitrary lengths. Since audio events may have arbitrary lengths,\na global max pooling layer is used to handle the variable-length in-\nput, instead of zero-padding. This makes the classifier model robust\nto audio events of arbitrary length.\nThe network is trained offline using both original audio clips\nas well as augmented audio clips where missing values are ran-\ndomly introduced. The augmented audio clips are imputed using\nthe method described in Section 5.3, before using them for training\nthe network. This is done to ensure that the model is robust to\nmissing audio segments.\n6 EVALUATION ON DATASETS\n6.1 Experimental Setup\nDataset. We use two popular audio classification datasets that con-\ntain audio clips of several seconds containing environmental sounds.\nWe also use two datasets containing short speech commands and\nphrases. We divided these datasets into multiple sub-classses de-\npending on the type of the sound. For example, ESC-50 [ 61] dataset\ncovers a large variety of sound types such as animal sound, human\nactivity, indoor noises etc. A brief summary of the datasets used\nin our study is shown in table 3. The duration of these clips are\n1–5 seconds. Since we want to demonstrate SoundSieve’s ability to\nclassify multi-second audio, we collected some of our own data by\nrecording as well as from online sources. Furthermore, we divided\nthe dataset according to the sound types.\nMobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland Mahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon\nDataset Clips Classes Examples\nUrbank8k [64] 8,732 10 Human, nature, mechanical.\nESC-50 [61] 2,000 50 Human activity, animal, indoor\nGSC [71] 105,829 35 Speech commands.\nFluent Speech [44] 23,132 31 Short phrases.\nTable 3: Datasets used in our study.\nModeling Intermittence. We use two parameters to model inter-\nmittence during our evaluation. First parameter is 𝐵which denotes\nthe maximum number of audio segments the system can process\nwithout power failure. In our experiments all the audio segments\nhave a length of 100𝑚𝑠. We also use another parameter 𝐶to denote\nthe ratio between charging and discharging time to process one\naudio segment of 100𝑚𝑠. In this way, we discretize the energy pat-\ntern of an intermittent system. We start with an initial budget of\n𝐵and each time we read and process audio segment of 100𝑚𝑠, we\ndecrease the value of 𝐵. Additionally, whenever the system skips\nan audio segment and waits in low-power mode, we increase the\nenergy budget by1\n𝐶. However since 𝐶can be floating point value,\nwe consider only the lower bound while checking for available\nenergy budget. Whenever the budget becomes zero, the system can\nnot sense and process an audio segment, even if the importance of\nthat segment is high.\nBaseline Solutions. We use three baseline solutions for compar-\nison: Vanilla, Periodic, and CIS [ 10]. In the vanilla approach, we\nemulate how an intermittent system normally works, i.e., sensing\nuntil the energy buffer is completely depleted and then waiting for\nit to be filled again. The periodic sampler samples every 𝑛th audio\nsegment, where 𝑛depends on the value of 𝐶. CIS [ 10] uses multiple\nsensor nodes to capture and classify 283ms audio. We implement\nthis approach by sensing 3 consecutive segments (3 ×100 = 300ms)\nand then waiting for the energy buffer to be full again. We denote\nthis baseline as CIS1 in our experiments.\nEvaluation Metrics. We use inference accuracy to quantify the\nperformance of the classifier. Accuracy refers to the portion of\ncorrectly classified instances on a dataset. We use detection of\nsound events for environmental sound classification dataset such\nas UrbanSound8k and ESC-50, and detection of certain phrases for\nGSC and Fluent Speech dataset. We also divide these dataset into\nmultiple categories depending on the types of sound.\n6.2 Comparison with the State-of-the-Art\nIn Figure 8, we compare SoundSieve’s performance with that of\nstate of the art approaches when the energy harvesting pattern is\nmodeled by setting 𝐶=1. We also show the original accuracy of the\nclassifier when there is no missing audio segment at all. Although\nthe classification accuracy drops when there are missing audio\nsegments due to power failure in an intermittent system, we see\nthat conserving energy to sense when there are more information\navailable improves the overall classification accuracy by 3%-17%\nthan other approaches.\nEffectiveness of Importance Predictor. In order to demonstrate\nwhy SoundSieve can produce better classification accuracy than\nother approaches, we show the average coverage of the most infor-\nmative segments in audio clips using both SoundSieve and vanilla\napproach in Figure 9. As we decrease the energy harvesting rate by\nsetting the value of 𝐶higher, we observe greater improvement in\ncovering only the most informative blocks compared to the vanilla\napproach.\n6.3 Effect of Energy Harvester\nWe change the energy harvesting pattern in our simulation by vary-\ning the value of 𝐶. A higher value of 𝐶refers to a larger amount\ntime required to harvest the energy of sensing one audio segment.\nIt also means that a higher percentage of audio is missing in the\naudio clip. Figure 10 demonstrates that as we decrease the energy\nharvesting rate, the classification accuracy drops slightly. How-\never since SoundSieve is able to cover more informative blocks\nas mentioned in 6.2, the drop in classification performance due to\nchange in energy harvesting pattern is minimal compared to other\napproaches.\nSoundSieve: Seconds-Long Audio Event Recognition on Intermittently-Powered Systems MobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland\nUrban8k ESC-indoor ESC-human ESC-animal Voice0204060Accuracy (%)C=1 C=1.5 C=2 C=2.5 C=3\nFigure 10: Effect of Harvesting Pattern\nIn Figure 11, we observe that SoundSieve achieves up to 25%\nhigher classification accuracy than the uniform sampling strategy\nof the vanilla approach.\nUrban8k ESC\n indoorESC\n humanESC\n animalVoice10203040506070Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla\n(a) C=1\nUrban8k ESC\n indoorESC\n humanESC\n animalVoice102030405060Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla (b) C=1.5\nUrban8k ESC\n indoorESC\n humanESC\n animalVoice102030405060Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla\n(c) C=2\nUrban8k ESC\n indoorESC\n humanESC\n animalVoice102030405060Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla (d) C=3\nFigure 11: Comparison of SoundSieve and Vanilla approach at differ-\nent energy harvesting rate\n6.4 Effect of Audio Length\n2.0 3.0 4.0\nLength (s)015304560Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla\n(a) C=1\n2.0 3.0 4.0\nLength (s)015304560Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla (b) C=1.5\n2.0 3.0 4.0\nLength (s)015304560Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla\n(c) C=2\n2.0 3.0 4.0\nLength (s)015304560Accuracy (%)SoundSieve Vanilla (d) C=3\nFigure 12: Effect of audio length at different energy harvesting rates.\nWe vary the length of audio clips from 2–4 seconds to test the\nrobustness of SoundSieve. This is because as the length of the audio increases, the\nintermittent system goes through more charge and discharge cy-\ncles, and consequently misses more audio segments. Sensing more\ninformative segments becomes crucial as the length of the audio\nincreases. This is evident in Figure 13 which compares their recall,\ni.e., the coverage of the most informative blocks by SoundSieve and\nthe vanilla. The recall for SoundSieve remains consistent for audio\nclips of all lengths while it gets worse for the vanilla.\nNote that 80%of the audio clips used in this experiment are about\n2 seconds long and there are very few audio clips that are more\nthan 3 seconds long. This creates an imbalance in the dataset and\ncauses a drop in the accuracy as the audio length increases.\n6.5 Effect of Segment Size\nC=1 C=1.5 C=2 C=3204060Accuracy (%)50 ms 100 ms 200 ms\nFigure 14: Performance of SoundSieve at different segment size\nSoundSieve analyzes and processes audio in the units of fixed-\nlength segments. In this experiment, we study how the segment\nsize affects SoundSieve’s classification accuracy under different\nenergy harvesting conditions. We use the ESC-50 [ 61] dataset for\nthis experiment. The result is summarized in Figure 14.\nWe observe that when the percentage of missing data is less,\ni.e.,𝐶≤1.5, the classification accuracy increases as we increase\nthe length of each segment. This is because, with larger segments,\nthe system gets a longer window of audio signals to compute the\nFourier transform and extract useful acoustic features. In contrast,\nwhen the percentage of missing data is very high, e.g., 𝐶>2, the\nsystem is highly likely to completely miss important segments\nif the segment length is large. Large segments give the system\nMobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland Mahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon\nless control over its sampling decisions, which causes a drop in\nclassification accuracy when a large portion of the data is missing.\nThis is why we see that the trend is reversed for higher values of\n𝐶in Figure 14. From this experiment, we learn that it is better to\nuse shorter segments when the percentage of missing data is very\nhigh, and larger segments when the percentage of missing data is\nlow in an intermittent system.\n6.6 Beyond Audio\nAlthough SoundSieve is specifically designed for audio signals,\nwe conduct a dataset-driven, limited-scale study to understand its\npotential in other types of signals.\nC=1 C=1.5 C=2 C=3015304560Accuracy (%)Our Approach Vanilla\nFigure 15: Comparison between our approach and the vanilla algo-\nrithm on UCI-HAR [4] dataset.\nWe use the UCI-HAR [ 4] dataset, which contains accelerometer\nand gyroscope readings from 30 participants performing six types\nof activities. The dataset is split into train ( 70%) and test ( 30%) sets.\nFigure 15 compares the classification accuracy of our approach\nwith that of the vanilla approach for different values of 𝐶. We\nobserve that our approach achieves 12%−20%more accuracy than\nthe Vanilla approach, and the gap increases when there are more\nmissing data – showing the resilience of the proposed technique in\nextreme energy harvesting conditions.\n7 SYSTEM IMPLEMENTATION\n7.1 Hardware Setup\nWe use TI MSP430FR5994 [ 2] MCU having 256KB FRAM, 8KB\nSRAM, direct memory access (DMA), and an operating voltage\nrange of 1.8V to 3.3V at 8MHz CPU clock speed.\nWe use both solar and RF harvesters. The solar panel has poly-\ncrystaline solar cells [ 25] that outputs up to 5V at 40mA, which is\nregulated to 3.3V using [ 1]. The RF harvester consists of a TX91501\npowercaster transmitter and a P2110 powerharvester receiver. A\n470𝜇𝐹capacitor is used for energy storage.\nA PMM-3738-VM1010-R piezoelectric MEMS microphone is used\nfor audio sensing. This microphone has a zero-power listening mode\nwhich allows it to continuously sense for audio event triggers at\nextremely low-power. Whenever an audio event is triggered, the\nmicrophone sends an interrupt to the MSP430 and the microphone\nis set to active listening mode. During this active mode, the system\ncontinuously samples audio at 5KHz, performs FFT, executes the\nfirst convolution layer of the classifier in the frequency axis and\nwrites the data to the FRAM using the low energy accelerator (LEA)\nand direct memory access (DMA).\nEnergy SourceSolar PanelVoltage RegulatorCapacitorMSP430MicrophoneMicrophone\nMSP430CapacitorRF TransmitterRF Receiver(a) Solar Harvester Setup\nEnergy SourceSolar PanelVoltage RegulatorCapacitorMSP430MicrophoneMicrophone\nMSP430CapacitorRF TransmitterRF Receiver (b) RF Harvester Setup\nFigure 16: Hardware Setup.\n7.2 Embedded System Software\nWe modify an open source Python tool [ 76] to convert a Tensor-\nFlow model to a C header file which contains the weights and the\nneural network architecture. This header file is combined with\nplatform-specific C implementation of neural network modules to\nobtain the audio classifier. We develop a complete C program that\nimplements audio sensing, pre-processing, sampling, imputation,\nand classifier modules, and cross-compile to produce executable\nbinary for the target system. We employ the static checkpoint calls\nsimilar to [ 3], where we profile the energy consumption at different\nstates of sensing and when the capacitor voltage drops below 2.2V,\nthe complete system state is copied to the FRAM.\n7.3 Microbenchmarking\nExecution Tracing. The intermittent system senses audio using a\nlow-power microphone while it harvests energy from a RF harvester.\nWe trace the operating voltage of the capacitor using a monitor [ 16].\nAfter sensing each segment, SoundSieve decides whether to sense\nthe next segment or to skip some segments, and wait in low-power\nmode to conserve energy and to fill up the energy buffer. This\ndecision is made based on the current energy level, global segment\nimportance, and local segment importance – predicted based on\nthe current segment that is being observed.\nA breakdown of all the steps of SoundSieve for classifying one\naudio clip is visualized in Figure 17. A breakdown of the sampling\ndecisions is shown in Table 4. Initially, when there is no audio event\nto sense, the microphone remains in the low-power mode, and the\nsystem periodically wakes up and checks its energy harvesting rate.\nUsing this information, a sampling strategy based solely on the\nglobal importance scores and energy harvesting rate is created. This\nstep corresponds to the first row of Table 4. When an audio event\ntriggers, the system wakes up and starts sensing and processing\nthe first audio segment. At the end of processing this segment, the\nsystem has the local importance scores of next 5 blocks as well as the\nglobal sampling strategy. If the local importance score of a segment\nis higher than a threshold and there is enough energy to spend,\nthe system samples that segment; otherwise, it waits for a more\ninformative segment according to the global sampling strategy. For\nexample, at time 𝑡2, even though the 𝑥5has the highest importance\nscore, the system decides to sense 𝑥3since its importance score was\ngreater than the pre-determined threshold. After the audio event\nSoundSieve: Seconds-Long Audio Event Recognition on Intermittently-Powered Systems MobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland\nends, the system starts executing the DNN to classify the audio\nevent.\nAudio\nSampling\nImpute \n+ Classify\nTime ( ms)Voltage (V )\nFigure 17: Execution steps of SoundSieve\nTime State Segments by Importance Decision\n𝑡0 init(𝑥2,𝑥7,𝑥5,𝑥8,𝑥1,···) Create Initial Sampling Strategy\n𝑡1 sample(𝑥2,𝑥5,𝑥3,𝑥6,𝑥4) Sense at𝑡2\n𝑡2 sample{𝑥5,𝑥3,𝑥7,𝑥4,𝑥6} Sense at𝑡3\n𝑡3 sample{𝑥7,𝑥4,𝑥8,𝑥5,𝑥6} Sense at𝑡4\n𝑡4 sample{𝑥5,𝑥7,𝑥8,𝑥9,𝑥6} Sense at𝑡5\n𝑡5 sample{𝑥8,𝑥7,𝑥9,𝑥10,𝑥6} Wait until𝑡7\n𝑡6 wait Wait until𝑡7\n𝑡7 sample{𝑥9,𝑥8,𝑥11,𝑥12,𝑥10} Wait until𝑡9\n𝑡8 wait Wait until𝑡9\n𝑡9 sample{𝑥13,𝑥12,𝑥11,𝑥10,𝑥14} Wait until𝑡12\n𝑡10 wait Wait until𝑡12\n𝑡11 wait Wait until𝑡12\n𝑡12 sample{𝑥13,𝑥16,𝑥14,𝑥17,𝑥15} Wait until𝑡13\n𝑡13 sample{𝑥14,𝑥18,𝑥15,𝑥16,𝑥17} Wait until𝑡18\nTable 4: Sampling strategy for an example audio clip\nOverhead Measurement. Table 5 summarizes the time and en-\nergy overhead breakdown of on-device audio processing. Sensing\nand sampling decisions are made sporadically throughout the dura-\ntion of the audio. The time overhead of the sampling algorithm is\nnegligible since FFT and feature extraction for sampling decision\nare computed in parallel. The DNN runs when the microphone\nsignals the end of audio. It takes less than a second (uninterrupted)\nto classify the audio—which can be interrupted 2-3 times due to\nintermittence.\nModule Time (ms) Energy (𝜇J)\nSensing + Preprocess 100 510\nSampling Decision 7 60\nDNN CONV (3 layers) 707 5850\nDNN FC layer 5 100\nTable 5: Execution time and energy overhead breakdown\n8 EVALUATION ON REAL SYSTEM\n8.1 Experimental Setup\nApplications. After validating the sampling technique, imputation\nmethod, and the classifier using dataset-driven experiments, we\ndesign two real-world deployment scenarios using both solar andradio frequency (RF) harvesters. We implement two realistic appli-\ncations: household activity detection, and voice phrase recognition.\nEach application has 10 classes of audio events and their lengths\nare shown in table 6. We use the DNN architecture shown in the\nTable 2 for classification. The DNN is split into three sub-networks\nto enable intermittent execution. After executing each sub-network,\nthe intermediate results are stored in the FRAM. Then the system\nwaits in the low-power mode while the capacitor charges, and peri-\nodically monitors the energy level. Finally, when enough energy is\naccumulated, the next sub-network of the DNN begins execution.\nApplication Classes Length (s)\nVoice Phrases 10 1-3\nHousehold Activities 10 2-4\nTable 6: Two applications used for real system deployment. Voice\nphrases include ten different phrases such as “go left\", “go right\" etc.\nHousehold activities include sound events such as brushing teeth,\npouring water, washing machine etc.\nMethodology. For each application, we randomly select 30 clips for\ntest – 1 clip for each of the 10 sound categories. We create a playlist\nwith these 30 clips and play each clip from a mobile device every\n10 second. Hence, the duration of the experiment is 300 seconds.\nPlaying the sounds from a mobile device makes it possible to repeat\nthe experiments and compare the results under different operation\nconditions such as mobility and energy variation.\nTo account for mobility and variation in harvested energy, we\ndivide the 300 seconds into three 100 seconds phases. In the first\nphase, we keep both the sound source and the energy source fixed\nrelative to the microphone. In the second and the third phases,\nwe move the sound source and the energy source, respectively, at\nconstant velocity. We use the solar setup for the voice application\nand the RF setup for the household application.\n8.2 Experimental Results\nEffect of Harvested Energy. In order to observe the effect of\nharvested energy we increase the distance between the RF harvester\nand the RF source when harvesting energy from RF, and change\nthe light intensity when harvesting energy from solar. In Figure 18\nbetween 100 seconds to 200 seconds, we observe that the classifier\nis able to maintain similar classification performance across both\napplication for varying energy harvesting rates. During this period,\nwe observe 4 out of 10 incorrect classifications for voice phrases\nand 3 out of 10 incorrect classifications for household activities,\nresulting in an accuracy of 60% and 67%, respectively.\nEffect of Sound Source Mobility. We vary the distance between\nthe sound source and the low-power microphone from 40cm to 60\ncm. The microphone is able to consistently wake up to the sound\nevent and the classification accuracy remains similar as shown in\nFigure 18 from 200 seconds to 300 seconds. During this period, we\nobserve 3 out of 10 incorrect classifications for voice phrases and 2\nout of 10 incorrect classifications for household activities, resulting\nin an accuracy of 67% and 80%, respectively.\nEffect of Audio Length. We experiment with audio clips of vary-\ning lengths – ranging from 1 second to 3 seconds for voice phrases,\nand 2 seconds to 4 seconds for household activity sounds. Since\nMobiSys ’23, June 18–22, 2023, Helsinki, Finland Mahathir Monjur, Yubo Luo, Zhenyu Wang, Shahriar Nirjon\nSolar Harveste r\n(mW)Sound Source \nDistance (cm)Incorrect \nClassification\n0   1   2  3  4   5      0    40   80  120 160 Time (s) Time (s)\nTime (s) Time (s)\nTime (s) Time (s)0               50             100             150            200            250           300    0               50             100             150            200            250           300    \n0               50             100             150            200            250           300    0               50             100             150            200            250           300    \n0               50             100             150            200            250           300    0               50             100             150            200            250           300    0  1   2  3   4  5      0    40  80  120 160 Incorrect \nClassificationSound Source \nDistance (cm)RFHarveste r\n(mW)\n(a) Voice Phrase Recognition (b) Household Activity Detection\nFigure 18: Effect of mobility and energy harvester in a real intermittent system.\nSoundSieve is able to identify more informative segments in an\naudio, the classification performance remains consistently similar\nfor audio clips of all lengths. Overall, we observe 10 out of 30 in-\ncorrect classifications for voice phrases and 7 out of 30 incorrect\nclassifications for household activities, resulting in an accuracy of\n67% and 76%, respectively.\n9 RELATED WORK\nIntermittent Computing. Intermittently powered systems experi-\nence frequent power failure that resets the software execution and\nresults in repeated execution of the same code, and inconsistency\nin non-volatile memory. Previous works address the progress and\nmemory consistency using software check-pointing [ 9,24,30,43,\n51,54,63,69,69], hardware interruption [ 5,6,55], atomic task-\nbased model [ 13,14,50], non-volatile processors (NVP) [ 48,49],\nand adaptive inference [36–38, 74].\nRecently SONIC [ 19,20,34] proposes a unique software sys-\ntem for intermittent execution of deep neural inference combining\natomic task-based model with loop continuation. None of the pre-\nvious works perform intelligent sampling like SoundSieve.\nModeling Energy Harvesting Systems. [65] analytically model\nthe trade-off associated with backing up data to maximize forward\npropagation. Even though energy harvesting system for a specific\nenergy source has been analyzed and modeled before [ 15,31,66],\nnone of the prior works focus on modeling the intermittence of\nsensor data on an intermittent system.\nIntermittent Audio Sensing. In [52] authors demonstrated audio\nsensing in energy harvested devices, however, they only sense\naudio events of 283ms in length, which is not adequate for many\nreal-world applications. In [ 39], authors simulated intermittency\nin audio sensing, however, they run post-processing and speech\nrecognition networks on high-end devices without considering any\ncomputation and energy constraints.Adaptive Informative Sampling. Resource constraint systems\noften need to monitor and sense large volume of data including\nvideo [ 8,57], RF [ 18,59] and audio [ 32]. In order to perform time\nand cost effective monitoring of the environment, many tiny robots\nand automated machines utilize adaptive informative sampling [ 18,\n32,33]. These robots model the environmental phenomena using\nGaussian process regression (GPR) [ 72]. However GPR is an useful\ntechnique for modeling spatial uncertainty, not temporal uncer-\ntainty.\n10 CONCLUSION\nIn this paper, we develop an intermittent audio sensing system that\nclassifies audio events that are longer than the discharging cycle\nof the system. We leverage inherent properties of audio signals to\ndesign a lightweight audio sampler, an imputation method, and\na classifier network to classify audio events. We show the effec-\ntiveness of the system using both dataset-driven and real-world\nevaluations. In the future, we plan to explore the possibility of\ndesigning content-aware intermittent sensing and classification\nsystems that deal with complex data such as camera images.\nACKNOWLEDGMENTS\nThis work was supported, in part, by grants NIH 1R01LM013329-01\nand NSF 2047461.\nREFERENCES\n[1] 2015.
