{"id": "2305.09315v1", "paper_content": "Neural Program Repair with Program Dependence\nAnalysis and Effective Filter Mechanism\nYuwei Zhang, Ge Li, Zhi Jin\nKey Laboratory of High Conﬁdence Software Technologies\n(Peking University), Ministry of Education\nBeijing, China\nfyuweizhang, lige, zhijin g@pku.edu.cnYing Xing\nSchool of Artiﬁcial Intelligence,\nBeijing University of Posts and Telecommunications\nBeijing, China\nxingying@bupt.edu.cn\nAbstract —Automated program repair is a crucial task for\nimproving the efﬁciency of software developers. Recently, neural-\nbased techniques have demonstrated signiﬁcant promise in gen-\nerating correct patches for buggy code snippets. However, most\nexisting approaches arbitrarily treat the buggy context without\nany analysis to capture the semantic relationship between the\nbuggy statement and its context. Additionally, we observe that\nexisting neural models may output an unaltered patch consistent\nwith the input buggy code snippet, which fails to be the correct\nhuman-written one for ﬁxing the given bug. To address the\naforementioned limitations, we present in this paper a novel\nneural program repair framework called REPEAT NPR , which\nadapts the general pre-trained language model for ﬁxing single-\nline Java bugs. We make the ﬁrst attempt to use program slicing\nto extract contextual information directly related to the given\nbuggy statement as repair ingredients from the corresponding\nprogram dependence graph and eliminate unaltered patches\nusing an intuitive but effective ﬁlter mechanism. We demonstrate\nthe effectiveness of REPEAT NPR on ﬁve benchmarks when\ncompared with state-of-the-art baselines.\nI. I NTRODUCTION\nProgram debugging is known to be an extremely labor-\nintensive and time-consuming task in the software develop-\nment and maintenance process [1]. To assist software develop-\ners in relieving manual debugging efforts, automated program\nrepair (APR), which aims at repairing defective programs\nwithout human intervention, has emerged as an important\nresearch area in both the software engineering (SE) and\nartiﬁcial intelligence (AI) communities. Over the past decade,\na variety of APR approaches [2]–[5] have been proposed to\nautomatically generate patches for bug ﬁxing. Among all tradi-\ntional approaches, template-based APR [6], [7], which utilizes\npredeﬁned patterns to transform buggy code snippets into ﬁxed\nones, is often regarded as state-of-the-art [8], [9]. However,\nexisting template-based approaches mainly design patterns for\nspeciﬁc bug types in a given programming language, which in\npractice necessitate professional domain knowledge to craft.\nTherefore, the manually designed templates are challenging\nto apply to unknown bug types or different programming\nlanguages, limiting the effectiveness of APR.\nAssisted by the powerful representation ability of deep\nlearning (DL) models, a recent trend is to leverage neural-\nbased techniques to automatically learn intricate relationships\nbetween buggy and ﬁxed code snippets from massive sourcecode corpus. Based on the naturalness hypothesis of soft-\nware code [10], the neural program repair (NPR) approaches\nintuitively formulate such a problem as the neural machine\ntranslation (NMT) task that translates defective programs\ninto correct versions [11], [12]. Generally, existing NMT-\nbased NPR systems adopt an encoder-decoder architecture\n[13], where the encoder extracts the hidden status of buggy\ncode snippets with their surrounding context, and the decoder\ngenerates ﬁxed code snippets based on the encoder’s hidden\nstatus. Compared with the previous APR approaches, the\nadvantage of NPR approaches lies in being less dependent on\nprofessional domain knowledge and additional artifacts (e.g.,\ntest suites). Consequently, researchers are concentrating on ad-\nvanced NPR approaches, which have shown great potential for\nautomatically generating patches using DL-based techniques.\nDespite achieving remarkable performance improvements,\nexisting NPR approaches still have some limitations. First,\nmost NPR models do not fully exploit the contextual infor-\nmation of buggy code snippets. It is vital to delineate what\ninformation in the source code should be included in the\nmodel input as repair ingredients. However, previous NPR\nmodels usually treat the notion of context pertaining to a buggy\nstatement within the corresponding source code in an arbitrary\nfashion, which neither captures the semantic relationship be-\ntween the buggy statement and its context nor encompasses\nessential ingredients for bug ﬁxing. For instance, some NPR\nmodels merely take the buggy statement [14] as input and\nignore context. Others utilize the enclosing buggy class [15]\nor the enclosing buggy method [11], [16], [17] as context. For\nsoftware developers, the context of a buggy statement plays a\nsigniﬁcant role in understanding the root cause of a bug and\ndetermining a potential bug-ﬁxing suggestion. Likewise, for\nthe DL-based NPR models, too much information in the input\nmay introduce noise that decreases the repair performance of\nthe model, and too little information may cause relevant repair\ningredient loss or overﬁtting issues. Thus, collecting contextual\ninformation related to the buggy code snippets as model input\nmay be more effective in generating correct patches.\nAnother drawback of current NPR approaches is that the\nprediction results of many NMT-based NPR models may be\nconsistent with the input buggy code snippets, which we call\nunaltered patches. The input buggy code snippets frequentlyarXiv:2305.09315v1  [cs.SE]  16 May 2023\nshare a highly similar vocabulary with the ﬁxed ones, which\nmay cause the model based on NMT architecture to output\nthe same results as the input. Figure 1 shows an example of a\nbuggy code snippet from an open-source GitHub project1. The\nbuggy statement (colored in red) with its context is regarded\nas the input for the corresponding NPR model, while the\nmodel prediction result (colored in gray) is identical to the\ninput buggy statement. Such an unaltered patch fails to be the\ncorrect human-written one (colored in green) for the given bug.\nThis phenomenon is unsupervised because it can determine\nwhether a model-generated patch is correct by comparing it\nwith the input buggy statement without knowing the ground\ntruth. Intuitively, when applying a different NPR model to the\nunaltered patches, there is a considerable possibility of ﬁxing\na portion of them and thus further reﬁning the APR process.\nFig. 1. An example of the unaltered patching phenomenon from a real-world\nopen-source software project in GitHub.\nIn summary, an effective NPR strategy should include\nadequate contextual information as repair ingredients for patch\ngeneration, while ﬁltering out the generated unaltered patches\nfor further processing. To that end, we present an ensemble\nNPR framework called R EPEAT NPR, which adopts p Rogram\ndEPE ndence Analysis with a ﬁl Ter mechanism, in this paper\nto address the limitations of existing approaches. Recently,\nlarge language models pre-trained on massive code corpus us-\ning code-aware objectives have yielded state-of-the-art results\non a vast number of code-related SE tasks. Inspired by this, the\nproposed framework adapts the general pre-trained language\nmodel CodeT5 [18] for ﬁxing bugs of single-line type, that is,\nwe adopt CodeT5 as the starting point to train R EPEAT NPR\nand ﬁne-tune it for the NPR task.\nSpeciﬁcally, we ﬁrst employ program slicing based on the\ndef-use analysis [19] to extract intra-procedural contextual in-\nformation in the form of source code token sequences directly\nrelated to a buggy statement as its repair ingredients from the\nprogram dependence graph (PDG) [20], [21], which explicitly\nrepresents both data and control ﬂow dependencies of the\ncorresponding buggy method. In addition, we collect global\ncontext according to the ingredients within the intra-procedural\ncontextual information. The slicing-based contextual informa-\ntion is in line with the process of program debugging by\n1https://github.com/alpha-asp/Alpha/commit/11c4adcsoftware developers. They commonly start by examining all\nthe ingredients (e.g., variables and method invocations) in the\nbuggy statement and then look through the code to ﬁnd out\nwhere those ingredients are deﬁned, used, and modiﬁed for\nunderstanding the bug [22]. Thus, such relevant contextual\ninformation is helpful for the DL-based models to better reason\nabout the bug-ﬁxing process. To tackle the second limitation,\nwe propose an ensemble approach that utilizes an intuitive\nﬁlter mechanism to combine different NPR models for bug\nﬁxing. The ﬁlter mechanism directly ﬁlters out the unaltered\npatches by comparing the former NPR model’s predicted\nresults with the input buggy code snippets and continues to\ninput the corresponding buggy code snippets into the next NPR\nmodel for processing. Just as the cross-checking form in the\ncode review scenario will ﬁnd more bugs than self-reviewing,\nan ensemble of different NPR models should also obtain better\nrepair performance than using a single NPR model.\nIn this paper, the proposed R EPEAT NPR framework focuses\non single-line bugs written in the Java programming language,\nwhich is the most popular task in previous studies [23]. To\nevaluate R EPEAT NPR, we ﬁrst select a large-scale dataset\ncalled BFP [11], consisting of single-line bugs that can be\nﬁxed by using single-line patches within the corresponding\nbuggy methods, as the source to ﬁne-tune the pre-trained\nmodel CodeT5. Extensive experiments are then conducted on\nﬁve benchmarks to validate the performance of R EPEAT NPR.\nThe experimental results demonstrate that R EPEAT NPR can\nachieve higher accuracy over the state-of-the-art baselines.\nThe main contributions of this paper are as follows:\n\u000fWe make the ﬁrst attempt at extracting slicing-based con-\ntextual information through program dependence analysis\nto enhance the NPR task.\n\u000fWe propose an ensemble framework that integrates dif-\nferent NPR models via an effective ﬁlter mechanism.\n\u000fWe conduct an extensive evaluation of ﬁve commonly\nused benchmarks to demonstrate the effectiveness of the\nproposed R EPEAT NPR framework.\nThe remainder of this paper is organized as follows. We\ndescribe the related work in Section II. Section III introduces\nin detail the proposed framework. We provide the experimental\nsetup in Section IV. Section V shows the analyzing results of\nour research. We disclose the threats to the validity of our\napproach in Section VI. Section VII draws conclusions and\nindicates directions for future work.\nII. R ELATED WORK\nAs a promising research topic, APR has received signiﬁcant\nattention from both the SE and AI communities. According\nto a living APR review [24], researchers have proposed a\nbunch of APR approaches in the last decade, which can be\ncategorized into two mainstreams: search-based [6], [7], [25]–\n[30] and semantics-based [31]–[35]. With the rapid develop-\nment of DL-based techniques, researchers have begun to pay\nmore attention to NPR approaches [36], [37], which have\ndemonstrated remarkable potential for improving program\nrepair performance. In contrast to traditional APR approaches,\nFig. 2. An overview of R EPEAT NPR.\nlearning-based techniques can automatically capture the se-\nmantic relationships between parallel bug-ﬁxing pairs.\nTufano et al. [11] harness the power of NMT to translate\nbuggy code snippets into ﬁxed ones. In particular, they abstract\nthe identiﬁers and literals in the source code to reduce the\nvocabulary size during the data preprocessing process. S E-\nQUENCE R [15] is an end-to-end approach based on sequence-\nto-sequence (Seq2Seq) learning that employs the long short-\nterm memory (LSTM) encoder-decoder architecture with copy\nmechanism to overcome the out-of-vocabulary issue. In addi-\ntion, S EQUENCE R considers the class-level abstract context\nsurrounding the buggy statement as input to capture the long-\nrange dependencies required for patch generation. Chakraborty\net al. [38] present a two-stage approach called CODIT to\nlearn code changes for ﬁxing the buggy statements by using\nan LSTM-based NMT model. DLFix [39] parses the source\ncode to AST and uses a tree-based LSTM to encode the code\nstructures surrounding the bug-ﬁxing changes as contextual\ninformation for learning code transformations. Lutellier et\nal. [16] propose an ensemble approach called CoCoNut that\ncombines convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and context-\naware NMT models to ﬁx bugs in multiple programming lan-\nguages. To better capture the diversity of bug-ﬁxing patterns,\nCoCoNut introduces a novel context-aware NMT architecture\nthat takes the buggy statement and its surrounding method\ncontext as two separate inputs. Different from the above NMT-\nor Seq2Seq learning-based models, Ding et al. [12] implement\nan edit-based model that performs token-level insertion and\ndeletion operations on the buggy code instead of generating\nraw tokens of the ﬁxed code. Zhu et al. [40] design Recoder,\na syntax-guided edit decoder with a novel provider/decider\narchitecture. Recoder receives the buggy statement with its\ncontext, the partial AST, and a tree path from the root node toa non-terminal node as inputs and embeds them using different\nencoders to generate edits on the AST of the buggy methods.\nInspired by the success of pre-trained models achieved in\nthe ﬁeld of natural language processing (NLP), an emerging\ntrend is to build language models pre-trained on large source\ncode corpus to boost the performance of program repair. Mas-\ntropaolo et al. [41] empirically investigate the performance of\nthe text-to-text transfer transformer (T5) model on four code-\nrelated tasks (including program repair). The experimental\nresults indicate that the T5 model can substantially boost the\nprogram repair performance. In a follow-up study of CoCoNut\n[16], Jiang et al. [42] propose CURE that modiﬁes the NMT-\nbased architecture by using a pre-trained GPT module to\nlearn contextual embedding. Mashhadi et al. [43] apply a pre-\ntrained model CodeBERT [44] to ﬁx simple Java bugs on the\nManySStuBs4J dataset [45].\nSpeciﬁcally, R EPEAT NPR utilizes the pre-trained language\nmodel to automatically learn bug-ﬁxing patterns from sliced\ncontext without necessitating professional domain knowledge.\nFurthermore, existing NPR models simply consider the context\narbitrarily. In contrast, R EPEAT NPR employs the program\ndependence analysis technique to enhance the NPR task by\nextracting slicing-based contextual information from PDG. At\nlast, R EPEAT NPR eliminates the unaltered patches via an\neffective ﬁlter mechanism that aims to take advantage of the\nensemble performance of different NPR models.\nIII. M ETHODOLOGY\nTo address the limitations mentioned in Section I, we\npresent the detailed design (as shown in Figure 2) of our\nproposed neural APR framework R EPEAT NPR in this section,\nwhich mainly consists of three stages: input processing, model\ntraining, and patch ﬁltering. We ﬁrst employ static program\nFig. 3. An example input for R EPEAT NPR.\nslicing on the PDG to extract the relevant contextual informa-\ntion of the corresponding buggy statement. Then, we leverage\na general pre-trained language model as the model skeleton\nto gain the powerful representation learning ability and ﬁne-\ntune it using the slicing-based contextual inputs. Finally, we\nintegrate different NPR models via an intuitive but effective\nﬁlter mechanism to ﬁlter out the generated unaltered patches.\nA. Input Processing\nExisting NPR approaches [11], [16], [17], [42], [46] usually\ntreat the context of a buggy statement in an arbitrary manner,\nsuch as by directly considering the whole buggy method or\na limited number of lines of code surrounding the buggy\nstatement, which neither captures the semantic relationship\nbetween the buggy statement and its context nor includes\nsufﬁcient contextual information as repair ingredients. Thus,\nwe propose to utilize program slicing techniques to extract\ncontextual information from the graphical representations of\nsource code (i.e., PDGs). Unlike existing approaches, we con-\nsider the control and data dependencies of the corresponding\nbuggy statement from the buggy method, which is reported to\nhelp capture the bug-ﬁxing patterns [22], [47].\n1) Program Dependence Analysis: The graphical represen-\ntations of source code are a fairly used data structure in the\nﬁeld of program analysis [48]. In this paper, we leverage PDG\nfor program analysis to examine the buggy statement and all\nthe ingredients (e.g., variable usages and method invocations).\nThe PDG explicitly represents the dependencies among state-\nments and predicates, which consists of two subgraphs: the\ndata dependence graph (DDG) and the control dependence\ngraph (CDG). The directed edges in DDG denote a data ﬂow\nfrom the source statement to the destination statement, reﬂect-\ning the inﬂuence of one variable on another (i.e., variable\ndeﬁnition, usage, or modiﬁcation). Similarly, each vertex in\nCDG has a control dependence on its parent vertex. Thus, the\ncontrol dependency edges reﬂect the inﬂuence of predicateson the values of variables. The upper left corner of Fig.3\ncorresponds to a buggy code snippet example, in which Line\n2 of the method parse in the EditCommandParser class\nis a buggy statement that needs to be ﬁxed. To illustrate, the\ndeﬁnition of argsTokenizer in statement S2can lead to\nits use in statement S6. In this case, a data dependence edge\nS299KS6is present in the PDG (shown on the right side of\nFig.3) of the buggy method.\n2) Dependency Context Extraction: To determine all con-\ntexts that affect the buggy statement, we introduce an algo-\nrithm for extracting dependency context based on the def-use\nanalysis of variables within the given node Nbuggy . Lines 2\nto 19 in the introduced algorithm detailed illustrate the steps\nof extracting contextual statements by leveraging program\nslicing (both backward and forward) and ingredient matching.\nSpeciﬁcally, we ﬁrst extract a set of variables that are accessed\ninNbuggy . For each variable varused in Nbuggy , we slice\na set of nodes Ncontext from PDG buggy , including both the\nnodes that have effect on varand those affected by var.\nThen, we examine all the ingredients in Ncontext and store\nthem in varUsageSet and methodInvocationSet respectively.\nIn addition, we collect all the public ﬁelds \feldSet and the\nsignatures of other public methods methodSignatureSet in\nthe buggy class from AST buggy . If the elements in \feldSet\normethodSignatureSet can match with any of the ingredients\ninNcontext , the corresponding AST node of such element is\nalso added to Ncontext as contextual information. The rationale\nthat motivated this inclusion is that generating a correct\npatch may require inspecting the status of the used public\nﬁelds or the argument list of the invoked public methods.\nFinally, the algorithm outputs the sliced context statements\nconcerning the slicing criterion (i.e., the buggy statement) as\nrepair ingredients to generate the inputs for R EPEAT NPR. In\nthe illustrated example of Fig.3, given the buggy statement\nS2, the algorithm extracts the sliced statements fS1,S3,\nS6,S7gas intra-procedural context and the public method\nparseTagsForEdit invoked in S7as global context. The\nextracted dependency context contains the repair ingredient\n\"PREFIX_DEADLINE\" , thus making it more likely to gen-\nerate the correct patch for ﬁxing the buggy statement.\n3) Context Statement Tokenization: At this stage, R EPEAT -\nNPR prepares the collected context statements in such a\nmanner that they can be directly fed into the DL models\nbased on an encoder-decoder architecture. The input of R E-\nPEAT NPR is composed of three parts: the buggy statement,\nthe intra-procedural context, and the global context. Existing\napproaches [16], [42] attempt to encode different parts of the\ninput separately and then fuse the encoding representations.\nHowever, it is still a challenge to effectively eliminate the\nsemantic gaps between different encoders and merge the\nseparated encoding vectors. Recently, the empirical results\n[49] indicate that the best strategy is to encode all of the\ndifferent input parts using a single encoder. In light of this,\nREPEAT NPR employs a consolidated format to combine the\nbuggy statement and its context into one sequence with special\nisolated tokens. As shown in the lower left corner of Fig.3,\nREPEAT NPR ﬁrst separates each content token by a single\nwhitespace. Then, different parts of the input are isolated\nby different abbreviate tokens (e.g., ‘‘<BOL>’’ denotes the\nbeginning of a buggy statement). R EPEAT NPR also inserts\na special token ‘‘<SEP>’’ for each sliced statement in the\nintra-procedural context to inform the end-of-line information.\nIn addition, R EPEAT NPR utilizes the sentence-piece tokenizer\n[50] to alleviate the open-vocabulary problem [51]. In this\nmanner, the low-frequency tokens can be synthesized more\neasily, thus making the program repair task more tractable.\nB. Model Training\nAs illustrated in the right side of Fig.2, R EPEAT NPR takes\nthe tokenized contextual sequences as input and works on threedifferent pieces of content: 1) the global context that contains\ninformation of public ﬁelds and method signatures; 2) the\nintra-procedural context sliced from the buggy method; 3) the\nbuggy statement. We begin with the pre-trained model CodeT5\n[18] serving as the reference architecture for the proposed\nREPEAT NPR framework. Then, we perform the ﬁne-tuning on\nthe task of program repair. At the inference step, the decoder\nsequentially predicts the candidate patches.\n1) Model Architecture: CodeT5 is a Seq2Seq language\nmodel pre-trained on the colossal clean crawled corpus, which\nconsists of an encoder and a decoder. In the context of NPR,\nan encoder takes a sequence of code tokens as input to map\nthe buggy context code X= [x1; : : : ; x m]into a ﬁxed-\nlength hidden state, while the decoder generates the output\nsequence of tokens Y= [y1; : : : ; y n]by taking the hidden state\nvector as input. Given an input sequence, CodeT5 ﬁrst obtains\nthe contextualized vector representations for input tokens by\nprojecting them into an embedded vector space through the\nembedding and positional encoding layer. Then, the vector\nis fed into the encoder to capture the long-term dependen-\ncies from different perspectives of the input sequences. The\nencoder comprises a stack of Transformer layers, each of\nwhich contains a multi-head self-attention layer followed by\na position-wise fully connected feed-forward network. The\ndecoder in CodeT5 is a Transformer-based sequential left-to-\nright decoder that generates one new token at a time until\na special stop token is reached. The decoder is similar in\nstructure to the encoder except for the usage of the mask\nmechanism in multi-head attention, which forces to attend only\nto past tokens and avoids distraction and information leakage\nof the subsequent tokens in training.\n2) Fine-Tuning: To learn generic bug-ﬁxing representa-\ntions, we leverage the pre-trained model CodeT5, which has\nachieved remarkable performance on a variety of NLP and\ncode-related SE tasks, as the starting point to train R EPEAT -\nNPR. We then ﬁne-tune the pre-trained model for the task of\nprogram repair. Fine-tuning techniques can optimize the pre-\ntrained parameters to make them more suitable for downstream\ntasks. Speciﬁcally, we represent the program repair task in a\n“text-to-text” format, where the input is the tokenized buggy\ncontextual sequence, and the output is the expected human-\nwritten patch. The ﬁne-tuning process is performed using the\ntraining corpus of BFP dataset D, and each instance within D\ncan be formally represented as a bug-ﬁxing pair di=fb; fg,\nwhere b= (c; m; s )comprises the global context obtained\nfrom the buggy class c, the intra-procedural context sliced\nfrom the buggy method mand the buggy statement s, and\nfdenotes the human-written patch. The ﬁne-tuning objective\nis to minimize the cross-entropy loss by learning the mapping\nb!fas a conditional probability p(fjb).\n3) Patch Inference: After ﬁne-tuning CodeT5 for the pro-\ngram repair task, R EPEAT NPR can learn latent bug-ﬁxing\npatterns and generate correct patches. During inference, R E-\nPEAT NPR represents the given buggy statement with three\npieces of token sequence: one for the given statement, the other\ntwo for its context. R EPEAT NPR follows the typical NPR\nprocess and employs beam search to generate the candidate\npatches sequentially. Once the decoder reaches the stop token,\nREPEAT NPR outputs the top-ranked patches for the given\nbuggy statement, where the number of generated candidate\npatches is conﬁgured as beam width.\nC. Patch Filtering\nThe lower left corner of Fig.2 shows the procedure of patch\nﬁltering. To avoid the unaltered patching issue existing in the\nNPR task, R EPEAT NPR proposes an intuitive mechanism to\nﬁlter out the unaltered patches generated by the pre-trained\nmodel CodeT5 for a given bug and then integrates a different\nNPR model into R EPEAT NPR to re-generate patches for the\nbug. As discussed in Section I, the unaltered patching issue\nfrequently occurs when using DL-based models for the APR\ntask since the buggy and ﬁxed code snippets are highly similar\nin the context of ﬁxing single-line bugs. Such an unsupervised\nphenomenon can be determined by simply comparing the\nmodel-generated patches with the input buggy code snippets.\nIn another word, we can directly ﬁlter out a portion of the\nincorrect patches without knowing the human-written ground\ntruth. The goal of patch ﬁltering is to ﬁlter out the unaltered\npatches from the generated candidate patches of the former\nmodel and utilize a new NPR model to work on them.\nSpeciﬁcally, given a buggy input, the candidate patches\ngenerated by CodeT5 in the ﬁrst stage can be divided into three\ncategories: correct patches (denoted as CPCodeT5 ), unaltered\npatches (denoted as UP CodeT5 ), and other incorrect patches that\nare inconsistent with the input buggy code snippet (denoted\nasIP CodeT5 ). Then, R EPEAT NPR ﬁlters out the UP CodeT5 and\nfeeds them into another NPR model in the next stage, whereas\ntheCPCodeT5 andIP CodeT5 remain as the ﬁnal suggested\npatches. At last, the new NPR model will continue to ﬁx\nthe buggy code snippets among UP CodeT5 with the newly-\ngenerated correct patches CPnewNPR . Thus, the correct patches\nproduced by R EPEAT NPR can be formulated as follow:\nCPRepeatNPR =CPCodeT5 +UP CodeT5\\ CP newNPR\nIV. E XPERIMENTAL SETUP\nA. Experimental Subjects\nTo evaluate R EPEAT NPR, we require a large-scale corpus\nof real-world bug-ﬁxing pairs to ﬁne-tune CodeT5. In this\npaper, we select the BFP dataset [11] as our original data\nsource, which consists of 787k bug-ﬁxing commits extracted\nfrom the GitHub repositories. Each instance is composed of\nboth the buggy and ﬁxed versions of a Java method. We\nfollow the steps described in Section III-A to construct our\nadapted dataset. First, we utilize PROGEX [52], a cross-\nplatform tool for extracting well-known graphical program\nrepresentations from source code, to parse each Java method\ninto a corresponding PDG for dependency context extraction.\nIn this step, we ﬁlter out the instances that failed to be parsed\nby PROGEX, meanwhile, we truncate the instances whose\nlength is longer than 512 after subword tokenization. Next,\nwe further split the BFP dataset into training, validation, andtesting sets by the ratio of 8:1:1. The dataset split is performed\ncarefully by taking into account possible data leakage. To be\nspeciﬁc, any two instances belonging to the same GitHub\nrepository cannot be put in two different sets (e.g., one in\ntraining and the other in testing). To enrich the diversity of\nbenchmarks for evaluation, we add four benchmarks widely\nused in the APR task to our testing set and perform the\nsame procedure of input processing. The detailed statistics for\nthe established benchmarks are listed in Table I. In total, we\ncollect 141195 bug-ﬁxing pairs in the training set, 13523 in\nthe validation set, and 13635 in the testing set. Note that each\ninstance represents a single-line bug that can be ﬁxed by using\na single-line patch within one Java method.\nTABLE I\nSTATISTICS OF THE FIVE BENCHMARKS .\nBenchmark Training Validation Testing\nBFP [11] 141195 13513 12224\nBugs.jar [53] - - 1000\nDefects4J [54] - - 260\nBears [55] - - 119\nQuixBugs [56] - - 32\nB. Experimental Design\nIn this section, we introduce the selected baselines, imple-\nmentation details, and how to assess the generated patches.\n1) Baseline: To evaluate the performance of our proposed\nframework, we compare R EPEAT NPR with the following ten\nbaselines that are related to our work:\n\u000fCODIT [38]: a two-stage approach that learns code\nchanges for bug ﬁxing by using a tree-based NMT model.\n\u000fEdits [12]: an edit-based model that performs token-level\ninsertion and deletion operations on buggy code.\n\u000fTufano [11]: an RNN-based model that can translate\nbuggy code snippets into ﬁxed ones.\n\u000fRecoder [40]: a syntax-guided decoder to generate edits\non the AST of the buggy method.\n\u000fCoCoNut [16]: an ensemble approach for ﬁxing bugs\nin multiple programming languages that combines CNNs\nand context-aware NMT models.\n\u000fSEQUENCE R[15]: a Seq2Seq learning-based approach\nfor end-to-end program repair that employs the LSTM\nencoder-decoder architecture with a copy mechanism.\n\u000fRoBERTa [57]: a Transformer-based model pre-trained\non a large corpus of natural language texts in a self-\nsupervised fashion.\n\u000fCodeBERT [44]: a bimodal pre-trained model for both\nprogramming and natural language that learns general-\npurpose representations to support code-related SE tasks.\n\u000fGraphCodeBERT [58]: a graph-based pre-trained model\nfor the programming language that considers data-ﬂow\ninformation along with code sequences.\n\u000fCodeT5 [18]: a pre-trained encoder-decoder model that\nbetter leverages the code semantics conveyed from the\ndeveloper-assigned identiﬁers.\n2) Implementation: We implement R EPEAT NPR with the\nopen-source framework PyTorch2and initialize R EPEAT NPR\nwith the pre-trained CodeT5-small checkpoint3from the Hug-\ngingface’s website. We adopt the same architecture as T5 [59]\nmodel, consisting of 8-headed attention and 6 layers in both\nthe encoder and decoder. We set the maximum source and\ntarget sequence lengths both to 512 and the batch size to 32.\nFor the implementation of the selected baseline models, we use\nthe publicly available source codes provided by the authors\nor the publicly released checkpoints. During the ﬁne-tuning\nstep, we train R EPEAT NPR for a maximum of 20 epochs.\nAfter each epoch, we compute the loss on the validation set\nand save the model with the minimum validation loss. To\navoid over-ﬁtting and save computation costs, we perform\nearly stopping if the validation performance does not improve\nfor ﬁve consecutive epochs. During the inference stage, we\nset both the beam size and candidate number to 10, which\nmeans that R EPEAT NPR will generate 10 top-ranked candi-\ndate patches for each perfectly fault-localized instance in the\ntesting set for validation. Prior studies usually choose a larger\ncandidate number for evaluation (e.g., Recoder [40] generates\n100 candidate patches). However, recent work [60] shows that\nmost developers are only willing to review up to 10 patches.\nThus, we also report the evaluation results within the top-10\ncandidates generated by each baseline in this paper to draw\nfair conclusions. During the patch ﬁltering step, we integrate\nSEQUENCE R, which is considered the best NPR model in a\nrecent empirical study [23], into R EPEAT NPR with our ﬁlter\nmechanism for patch re-generation. We conduct experiments\non 4 Nvidia GTX 1080Ti GPUs of 12 GB memory.\n3) Patch Assessment: Existing APR approaches typically\nuse test suites for patch validation, which run the human-\nwritten test suite against each candidate patch to ﬁnd plausible\npatches that can pass all the test cases. Due to the overﬁtting\nissue, such plausible patches need further manual assessment\nto conﬁrm their correctness. However, the empirical results\n[61] indicate that manual patch assessment 1) needs expertise\nto understand the semantics of the program under repair, 2)\nmay introduce biases to some extent, and 3) can be undoable\nwhen the scale is large. To avoid human bias and reduce\nmanual effort, we use a more objective way in our experiment\nto assess the correctness of each generated candidate patch by\nchecking if it is identical to the human-written one, that is, we\nuse the exact match metric to evaluate the model performance\non the testing set in this paper.\nV. R ESULTS AND ANALYSIS\nIn this section, we present the experimental results for\nmeasuring the performance of our proposed framework and\nanswering the following three research questions (RQs):\n\u000fRQ1 : How does R EPEAT NPR perform compared with\nthe state-of-the-art baselines?\n\u000fRQ2 : How does each component in R EPEAT NPR impact\nthe performance of bug ﬁxing?\n2https://pytorch.org\n3https://huggingface.co/Salesforce/codet5-small\u000fRQ3 : What is the quality of the candidate patches gen-\nerated by R EPEAT NPR?\nA. Answering RQ1\nTo answer this question, we compare R EPEAT NPR with ten\nstate-of-the-art baselines on ﬁve commonly used benchmarks.\nTo make a fair comparison, we uniformly use the training set\nof BFP to train or ﬁne-tune the baselines with corresponding\ninput representations via the same training strategy and hyper-\nparameter settings described in Section IV-B2.\n1) Experimental Metric Evaluation: Table II reports the\nnumber of correct patches that are identical to the human-\nwritten ground truth generated by R EPEAT NPR and the ten\nbaselines. The best result for each benchmark is marked in\nbold. As shown in Table II, R EPEAT NPR substantially outper-\nforms the ten baselines on all ﬁve benchmarks. Speciﬁcally,\nREPEAT NPR produces more correct patches than the best\nbaseline model CodeT5 by 15.9% in the BFP benchmark,\n12.0% in the Bugs.jar benchmark, 22.2% in the Defects4J\nbenchmark, 62.5% in the Bears benchmark, 7.1% in the\nQuixBugs benchmark. As exact match is a strict metric,\nsuch improvements prove the superiority of R EPEAT NPR in\nthe NPR task. We also observe that the pre-trained models\nare more promising than those trained from scratch. For\ninstance, CodeT5 improves S EQUENCE R by 14.3% in the\nBFP benchmark and 51.5% in the Bugs.jar benchmark. Since\nthe two models have a similar architecture and a comparable\namount of parameters, such improvements demonstrate that\nusing pre-training techniques is advantageous for learning bug-\nﬁxing patterns for patch generation. Additionally, the Seq2Seq\nlearning-based models (i.e., R EPEAT NPR, CodeT5, and S E-\nQUENCE R) can obtain better results than other baselines,\nwhich illustrates the equal importance of correctly understand-\ning the buggy input and generating the candidate patches.\nTABLE II\nCOMPARISON OF REPEAT NPR AGAINST THE BASELINES .\nModelBFP Bugs.jar Defects4J Bears QuixBugs\n12224 bugs 1000 bugs 260 bugs 119 bugs 32 bugs\nCODIT 44 5 5 1 1\nEdits 117 37 8 2 6\nTufano 621 56 18 8 7\nRecoder 1003 61 33 1 10\nCoCoNut 1601 66 37 16 13\nSEQUENCE R 1917 99 38 14 15\nRoBERTa 1373 103 21 8 8\nCodeBERT 1686 111 29 12 7\nGraphCodeBERT 1635 115 25 12 6\nCodeT5 2191 150 36 16 14\nREPEAT NPR 2540 168 44 26 15\n2) The Impact of Fixing Rate under Different Candidate\nNumbers: We further investigate the ﬁxing rates of each corre-\nsponding model under different candidate numbers on the BFP\nbenchmark (12224 bugs). We set the candidate numbers from\n1 to 10, and the ﬁxing rate denotes the ratio of exact match\npredictions. Note that ﬁxing one bug under candidate number\nk indicates that at least one of the k model-generated candidate\npatches is identical to the human-written ground truth. As it\nFig. 4. The ﬁxing rates of each NPR model under different candidate numbers.\ncan be seen in Fig.4, R EPEAT NPR consistently outperforms\nthe ten baselines under all candidate numbers on the BFP\nbenchmark. As the candidate number increases, we notice\nthat the performance gains obtained by different NPR models\nmay be inconsistent, even if they are trained with the uniﬁed\ndataset. For example, when the candidate number is limited\nto 1, Recoder relatively outperforms S EQUENCE R by 21.4%\nin terms of the ﬁxing rate. But when the candidate number\nreaches 10, the number of correct patches that S EQUENCE R\ncan generate is almost twice that of Recoder.\n3) Answer to RQ1: In summary, R EPEAT NPR signiﬁcantly\noutperforms the baselines in terms of the exact match metric.\nSuch improvements demonstrate the effectiveness of R EPEAT -\nNPR in the APR task. Our observations also indicate that\nREPEAT NPR is capable of generating more correct patches\nunder different candidate numbers than the baselines.\nB. Answering RQ2\nTo answer this question, we evaluate the impact of differ-\nent components (i.e., program dependence analysis and ﬁlter\nmechanism) in the design of R EPEAT NPR by conducting\nablation experiments. For a fair comparison, the training\nstrategy and the hyper-parameter settings are consistent with\nthose described in Section IV-B2.\n1) Ablation Study: Table III lists the results with each row\nrepresenting one model and the number of correct patches that\nsuch a model can generate for each benchmark. The best result\nis marked in bold. To show how each component improves the\nnumber of correctly generated patches, we start with the basic\npre-trained model CodeT5, which is ﬁne-tuned on the original\nBFP dataset without using program dependence analysis A\nand ﬁlter mechanism F. When we augment CodeT5 with\ncomponent F, the ablated model CodeT5 Frespectively repairs\n88, 6, 1, 3, and 0 more bugs for the ﬁve benchmarks. When\nwe add component Ato CodeT5, the number of correct\npatches generated by the ablated model CodeT5 Afor the\nﬁve benchmarks is respectively 240, 8, 6, 7, and 1 more\nthan that of CodeT5. Generally, the correct patches generated\nby the three ablated models are fewer than R EPEAT NPR,\nwhich demonstrates the necessity of each component. Figure 5depicts a bug-ﬁxing example from BFP only correctly patched\nby R EPEAT NPR. In this case, the predicate of the buggy\nstatement is redundant according to its context. Thus, a correct\npatch needs to remove the entire body of the if statement.\nWe can observe that the two ablated models without program\ndependence analysis (i.e., CodeT5 and CodeT5 F) generate\nthe same incorrect patch that is semantically equivalent with\nthe buggy statement, whereas CodeT5 Agenerates an unal-\ntered patch. Therefore, by further applying component Fon\nCodeT5A, REPEAT NPR can successfully produce the correct\npatch that is identical to the human-written one.\nTABLE III\nABLATION STUDY FOR REPEAT NPR.\nModelComponent Benchmark\nA F BFP Bugs.jar Defects4J Bears QuixBugs\nCodeT5 /remove /remove 2191 150 36 16 14\nCodeT5 F /remove /ok 2279 156 37 19 14\nCodeT5 A /ok /remove 2431 158 42 23 15\nREPEAT NPR /ok /ok 2540 168 44 26 15\nFig. 5. An example from BFP only repaired by R EPEAT NPR.\n2) The Impact of Program Dependence Analysis on Pre-\nTrained Models: We further investigate the ﬁxing rate of each\npre-trained model when using dependency context as input\non the BFP benchmark. Table IV presents the comparison\nresults with each pre-trained model comprising two lines of\nexperimental results, in which the ﬁrst line shows the results\nof the model that is ﬁne-tuned without using the contextual\ninformation based on the program dependence analysis, and\nthe second line shows the results of using such information.\nThe Fix@k metric denotes that a correct patch for a given\nbug should be among the top-k generated ones. As shown\nin Table IV, we can observe that providing the dependency\ncontext as repair ingredients contributes to improving the bug-\nﬁxing performance of all the pre-trained models. Among the\nfour pre-trained models, CodeT5 gains the most performance\nimprovements from the input contextual information. This is\nbecause CodeT5 is an encoder-decoder model that can lever-\nage the code semantics conveyed from the developer-assigned\nidentiﬁers to better derive generic representations, whereas the\nencoder-only pre-trained models (e.g., CodeBERT) treat the\nsource code in the same way as natural language, neglecting\nthe special characteristics of code. Figure 6 demonstrates\na bug that can only be ﬁxed by CodeT5 A. As it can be seen in Table V, the ﬁxing rates decreasesimultaneously when exchanging the models’ order, which\nindicates that the order change may affect the performance\nof bug ﬁxing. Speciﬁcally,\ncollecting the dependency context as repair ingredients can\nbetter exploit the powerful representation learning ability of\nthe pre-trained model. Meanwhile, the proposed ﬁlter mech-\nanism can further increase the number of correct patches by\nmaking use of the ensemble performance of different NPR\nmodels to eliminate the generated unaltered patches.\nC. Answering RQ3\nTo answer this question, we manually inspect the generated\ncandidate patches for further evaluation. The evaluation is\nsplit into two aspects: 1) analyzing the bug types ﬁxed in\nthe generated correct patches; 2) discussing the overlapping\nphenomenon among the generated correct patches.\n1) Bug Types Evaluation: We ﬁrst compute the difference\nbetween two ASTs generated by Spoon [62] using the Gumtree\nalgorithm [63]. In total, we classify the bugs into four types,\nthat is Simple Delete, Simple Insert, Simple Replace, and\nMixed, according to the edit operations needed to transform\none buggy statement into its ﬁxed version. Table VI lists the\ncomparison results with each row representing one model and\nthe number of correct patches that such a model can generate\nfor each bug type on the BFP benchmark. The best result\nis marked in bold. The statistic results shown in Table VI\nindicate that existing NPR models do well in ﬁxing bugs that\nonly need deletion edit operations, but are weak in ﬁxing\nthe more complex ones. That makes sense because the insert\nand replace edit operations require the model to search for\nadditional tokens to ﬁx the given bug. Speciﬁcally, Recoder\nperforms the best on generating code-removal patches, while\nREPEAT NPR outperforms all the baselines in ﬁxing the other\nFig. 7. The overlapping patching rates and unique patch numbers of the eleven NPR models.\nthree types of bugs. Overall, R EPEAT NPR can achieve better\nor comparable performance than the baseline models in ﬁxing\nboth simple and complex bugs.\nTABLE VI\nCOMPARISON OF THE FIXED BUG TYPES BETWEEN REPEAT NPR AND THE\nBASELINES .\nModelSimple Delete Simple Insert Simple Replace Mixed\n1020 bugs 2274 bugs 4508 bugs 4422 bugs\nCODIT 2 (0.20%) 2 (0.09%) 39 (0.87%) 1 (0.00%)\nEdits 16 (1.57%) 23 (1.01%) 62 (1.38%) 16 (0.36%)\nTufano 114 (11.18%) 121 (5.32%) 318 (7.05%) 68 (1.54%)\nRecoder 503 (49.31%) 73 (3.21%) 282 (6.26%) 145 (3.28%)\nCoCoNut 246 (24.12%) 399 (17.55%) 652 (14.46%) 304 (6.87%)\nSEQUENCE R 391 (38.33%) 419 (18.43%) 839 (18.61%) 268 (6.06%)\nRoBERTa 279 (27.35%) 258 (11.35%) 687 (15.24%) 149 (3.37%)\nCodeBERT 283 (27.75%) 361 (15.88%) 797 (17.68%) 245 (5.54%)\nGraphCodeBERT 302 (29.61%) 317 (13.94%) 795 (17.64%) 221 (5.00%)\nCodeT5 273 (26.76%) 560 (24.63%) 1000 (22.18%) 358 (8.10%)\nREPEAT NPR 357 (35.00%) 623 (27.40%) 1104 (24.49%) 456 (10.31%)\n2) Overlapping Phenomenon Evaluation: As illustrated in\nFig.7, each row lists the overlapping ratio of correct patches\ngenerated for the BFP benchmark between one NPR model\nand the other models, while the diagonal indicates the number\nof unique correct patches generated by each model. For\ninstance, 52% of the correct patches generated by R EPEAT -\nNPR (row 12) can also be ﬁxed by S EQUENCE R (column\n7). And there are 390 bugs (row 12, column 12) that can\nonly be ﬁxed by R EPEAT NPR. According to the results in\nFig.7, we observed that the better the repair performance of\na model, the higher the overlapping patching rate between it\nand other models. Regarding the results in Table II, we can\nﬁnd that our proposed framework R EPEAT NPR, CodeT5, and\nSEQUENCE R are the top three models evaluated on the BFP\nbenchmark. Relatively, the overlapping rate of other models\nwith the three ones is much higher. The possible reason for\nthis phenomenon may be that the DL-based approaches mostly\nadopt similar network architectures and inference paradigms.\nFurthermore, the number of unique correct patches generated\nby R EPEAT NPR is larger than that of other baselines (except\nRecoder). As is discussed in Section II, the possible reason is\nthat Recoder is designed to generate edits on the AST of thebuggy methods, which is different from the NMT- or Seq2Seq\nlearning-based approaches. Nevertheless, regarding the results\nwe obtained in Table VI, Recoder tends to ﬁx bugs of a speciﬁc\ntype (i.e., Simple Delete).\nVI. T HREATS TO VALIDITY\nIn this section, we illustrate the main threats to the validity\nof our approach, which are listed as follows:\n\u000fExternal threat : The quality of the selected experimental\nsubjects and the generalizability of R EPEAT NPR are the\nprincipal threats to external validity in this paper. First,\nwe use the mainstream dataset BFP for ﬁne-tuning as\nprevious studies [11], [18], [23], [64]. We remove all\nduplicate instances between the training and testing sets\nto avoid the data leakage issue. Second, R EPEAT NPR\nhas been evaluated in Java bugs. Besides, the designed\ncomponents in R EPEAT NPR are language-agnostic and\ncan be applied to other programming languages.\n\u000fInternal threat : It is widely known that DL-based mod-\nels are sensitive to hyper-parameters. Thus, using a sub-\noptimal hyper-parameter can pose an internal threat to\nthe validity of R EPEAT NPR. Due to the limitation of\ncomputational resources, we cannot thoroughly explore\noptimal hyper-parameters in this paper. Since Raffel et al.\n[59] have explored effective settings of hyper-parameters\nthrough extensive experiments in previous work, we\nuse the same hyper-parameters described in their paper.\nWe acknowledge that there might be room for further\nimprovement through additional tuning.\n\u000fConstruct threat : In this paper, the experimental metric\nused for model evaluation is referred to as the construct\nthreat. We adopt the exact match metric to assess the\ncorrectness of the generated candidate patches. Although\nsuch a metric does not represent human judgment, it is\na strict and objective metric that can be used to quickly\nand quantitatively evaluate the model performance. In the\nfuture, we will conduct more human evaluations.\nVII. C ONCLUSION AND FUTURE WORK\nIn this paper, we propose a novel NPR framework called\nREPEAT NPR that adapts a state-of-the-art encoder-decoder\nlanguage model for ﬁxing single-line Java bugs. To accurately\ncapture the semantic relationship between the buggy state-\nment and its context, we make the ﬁrst attempt to leverage\nprogram dependence analysis to improve the NPR task by\nextracting slicing-based contextual information as repair in-\ngredients from PDG. Additionally, we propose an intuitive but\neffective ﬁlter mechanism to eliminate the unaltered patches\nby taking advantage of the ensemble performance of different\nNPR models. Empirical results demonstrate that R EPEAT NPR\noutperforms the state-of-the-art approaches on ﬁve widely\nused benchmarks in terms of the exact match metric. In the\nfuture, we plan to expand the program analysis techniques to\ncollect project-speciﬁc knowledge for bug ﬁxing. Since the\nPDG and AST exist in most programming languages, we can\nextend R EPEAT NPR with small modiﬁcations to support new\ntarget languages. Furthermore, we will design a better ﬁlter\nmechanism that can determine more incorrect patches (except\nthe unaltered ones) without comparing them with the human-\nwritten ground truth.\nREFERENCES\n[1] M.
